Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
9 min
The Agent in Pre-emption Litigation
The role of an agent in pre-emption litigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an agent who purchases property is a litigant for the pre-emptor until delivery to the principal, and compare it with the opposing view.

Core Idea
The agent is the contractor, so he is the litigant until delivery; after delivery, the principal takes over.

When a property is bought through an agent, the pre-emptor may need to litigate. Who is the proper adversary – the agent or the principal? The Hanafi school has a specific rule based on the agent's role as the actual contractor. This lesson explains the duration of the agent's status as litigant and the opposing view of the three imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
The agent in purchase (wakīl bil-shirā') is a proper litigant for the pre-emptor.
This is because the agent is the one who actually entered the contract.
2
Determine the Duration
The agent remains a litigant only as long as he has not delivered the property to the principal. After delivery, he loses possession and ownership, and is no longer a litigant.
The phrase 'ما لم يسلّم إلى الموكل' – as long as he has not delivered.
3
Explain the Reasoning
Litigation is a right of the contract, and the contractor (whether principal or agent) is the one who connected with the contract. The agent is the contractor here.
This is similar to the seller who remains a litigant until delivery.
4
State the Opposing View
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i hold that the principal is the litigant from the outset, because the agent is merely a messenger (safīr).
For them, the contract's rights attach to the principal immediately.
5
Consider the Exception
If someone buys for another without being appointed as agent (al-mushtarī li-ghayrihi), he is also a litigant until delivery. The text notes that the author could have included this rule.
From al-Tatarkhaniyya: a person who buys for another without his command is a litigant until he delivers the property.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on Kitab al-Shuf'ah from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering several distinct rulings. First, the pre-emptor may also litigate with the seller directly if the property is still in the seller's possession, because the seller has an original right of possession. This is different from a depositary or borrower, who have only derivative possession and thus are not proper litigants. Second, the judge does not hear the pre-emptor's evidence unless the buyer is present, because both the seller and buyer have rights in the property. After the buyer is present, the judge rescinds the sale. If the property has already been delivered to the buyer, the seller's presence is not required. Third, the liability for the price (al-uhdah) – i.e., if the pre-emptor's ownership is later invalidated, who must return the price? According to the Hanafi school, before delivery, the liability is on the seller, because the contract's rights are with the contracting party. The 'three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i) hold that liability is on the buyer, because rights revert to the owner. This is a significant inter-madhhab dispute. Fourth, an agent who buys for a principal is a proper litigant for the pre-emptor as long as the agent has not delivered the property to the principal. After delivery, the principal becomes the litigant. This is because the agent is the actual contractor. The opposing view (the three) holds that the principal is the litigant from the start, as the agent is merely a messenger. Fifth, the pre-emptor has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) and option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), even if the buyer had stipulated exemption from defects. This is by consensus, because the pre-emptor is taking by a new purchase, so he has the same options as any buyer. His options are not affected by the buyer's actions. Sixth, if the pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price, the buyer's word is taken with an oath, because he is the denier of the pre-emptor's claim. They do not both swear (tahaluf) because mutual oaths are only for cases where both parties deny each other's claims.

Key Terms
وكيل بالشراء (wakil bil-shira'): An agent appointed specifically to purchase a property on behalf of a principal.
موكل (muwakkil): Principal – the person who appoints an agent.
سفير (safīr): Messenger or intermediary; used by the opposing imams to describe the agent's role.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how long does an agent remain a proper litigant for the pre-emptor?
A) Until the contract is signed
B) Until he delivers the property to the principal
C) Until the principal pays the price
D) Forever, even after delivery

Key Takeaway
The agent is the litigant until delivery of the property to the principal, because he is the contractor; after delivery, the principal becomes the litigant.

Pre-emptor's Options and Price Dispute
Pre-emptor's options and resolution of price disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the pre-emptor has independent options of sight and defect, and explain the rule for resolving price disputes between pre-emptor and buyer.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor's options are independent of the buyer's actions; in price disputes, the buyer's word is accepted with oath.

When a pre-emptor takes property through pre-emption, he becomes a buyer in his own right. He enjoys the standard options of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) and defect (khiyar al-'ayb) just like any other buyer. Furthermore, if he and the buyer disagree on the price, a specific procedure applies. This lesson covers these two important practical issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Pre-emptor's Options
The pre-emptor has khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect), even if the buyer had previously waived defects.
This is by consensus (bil-ijmā‘).
2
Explain the Independence
The pre-emptor's options are not affected by the buyer's actions, because the pre-emptor is not the buyer's representative; he is taking by a new, independent purchase.
The text says: 'الشفيع ليس بنائب عنه' – the pre-emptor is not his representative.
3
Identify the Price Dispute Rule
If the pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price, the buyer's word is accepted (with his oath) because he is the denier of the pre-emptor's claim.
The pre-emptor claims a lower price, the buyer claims a higher price; the buyer denies the lower price.
4
Explain the Absence of Mutual Oaths
They do not perform tahaluf (mutual oaths) because that is only for cases where both parties deny each other's claims. Here only the buyer denies.
Tahaluf is established by text (Quran or Sunnah) only when denial is on both sides.

Source Text

(عيني) ولا يسمع البينة إلخ أي لا يسمع القاضي البينة حتى يحضر المشتري؛ لأن لكل منهما في المبيع حقا، للبائع اليد وللمشتري الملك، والشفيع يتعرض للحقين جميعا، فلابد من حضورهما [رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۸/۲] فيفسخ البيع إلخ : أي فيفسخ القاضي البيع بمشهد المشتري وحضوره، بخلاف ما بعد القبض حيث لا يشترط حضور البائع؛ لأن العقد قد انتهى بالتسليم، فصار البائع أجنبيا عنهما. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۳٨/٢] والعهدة على البائع إلخ: أي ضمان الثمن عند الاستحقاق على البائع عندنا أي قبل تسليم البيع إلى المشتري، وقالت الثلاثة على المشتري، والأصل في ذلك أن حقوق العقد ترجع إلى المالك عندهم، وإلى العاقد عندنا. (عيني، فتح) والوكيل بالشراء إلخ [ إذا وكل رجلا ليشتري له دارا فاشترى فالوكيل خصم للشفيع. (ملا مسكين)] فلا يشترط للقضاء حضور الموكل ولا كذلك البائع؛ لأنه ليس بنائب عن المشتري، فلا يكتفي بحضوره، بخلاف الوكيل والأب ووصيه كالوكيل. (فتح) ما لم يسلم إلى الموكل: أي الوكيل بالشراء خصم للشفيع ما لم يسلم إلى الموكل؛ لأن الخصومة فيه من حقوق العقد، وهي إلى العاقد أصيلا كان أو وكيلا، ولهذا لو كان البائع و وكيلا للشفيع أن يخاصمه، ويأخذها منه بحضور المشتري، كما إذا كان البائع هو المالك إلا أنه إذا سلمها إلى الموكل، لا يد للوكيل، ولا ملك له، ولا يكون خصما بعده، فصار كالبائع، فإنه يكون خصما ما لم يسلمه إلى المشتري، فإذا سلمها إليه لم يبق له يد، ولا ملك، فيخرج من أن يكون خصما تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٠/٨] وعند الثلاثة الخصم هو الموكل ابتداء؛ لأن الوكيل بمنزلة السفير. [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٣٨/۲] وفي "التاتارخانية": لو اشترى لغيره بغير أمره، فهو خصم ما لم يسلم العين إلى من اشتراها له، فلو قال المؤلف: "والمشتري لغيره خصم ما لم يسلم لكان أولى تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٠/٨] وللشفيع خيار الرؤية إلخ أي يثبت للشفيع خيار الرؤية، وخيار العيب وإن شرط المشتري البراءة من العيب، وهذا بالإجماع؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة شراء من المشتري إن كان الأخذ بعد القبض، وإن كان قبله فهو من البائع، فيثبت له الخيار تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦١/٨] ولا يسقط الخيار برؤية المشتري، وبشرط براءته؛ لأن الشفيع ليس بنائب عنه، فلا يسقط حقه بإسقاط المشتري. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۸/۲] وإن اختلف إلخ أي إذا اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في مقدار الثمن، فقال الشفيع اشتريتها بمائة، وقال المشتري: اشتريتها بمائة وعشرين، فالقول للمشتري؛ لأن الشفيع يدعي عليه استحقاق الأخذ عند نقد الأقل، والمشتري ينكر ذلك، والقول للمنكر مع يمينه ولا يتحالفان؛ لأن التحالف عرف بالنص فيما إذا وجد الإنكار الجانبين.
'And he litigates with the seller etc.': meaning the pre-emptor may litigate with the seller if the sold property is in his hand, because he has a rightful possession originally, so he is a litigant like the owner, unlike the depositary and borrower and similar, because their possession is not original, so they are not litigants. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/259) 'And the evidence is not heard until the buyer is present, then the sale is rescinded in his presence, and the liability (for the price) is on the seller, and the agent in purchase is a litigant for the pre-emptor as long as he has not delivered to the principal, and the pre-emptor has the option of sight and defect, even if the buyer stipulated exemption from defect, and if the pre-emptor and buyer dispute over the price.' (Ayni) 'And the evidence is not heard etc.': meaning the judge does not hear the evidence until the buyer is present, because each of them has a right in the sold property: the seller has possession and the buyer has ownership, and the pre-emptor is contesting both rights, so their presence is necessary. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 3/338] 'Then the sale is rescinded etc.': meaning the judge rescinds the sale in the presence and attendance of the buyer, unlike after delivery where the seller's presence is not required, because the contract has ended with delivery, so the seller has become a stranger to them. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 3/338] 'And the liability is on the seller etc.': meaning the guarantee of the price upon eviction is on the seller in our view (i.e., before delivery of the sale to the buyer), and the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i) say it is on the buyer. The basis is that rights of the contract revert to the owner according to them, and to the contractor according to us. (Ayni, Fath) 'And the agent in purchase etc.': [if a man appoints an agent to buy a house for him and he buys it, the agent is a litigant for the pre-emptor. (Mulla Miskin)] So the presence of the principal is not required for judgment, nor is the seller; because he is not a representative of the buyer, so his presence is not sufficient, unlike the agent, the father, and his executor, who are like the agent. (Fath) 'As long as he has not delivered to the principal': meaning the agent in purchase is a litigant for the pre-emptor as long as he has not delivered to the principal, because the litigation is among the rights of the contract, and it pertains to the contractor, whether he is an original or an agent. For this reason, if the seller is an agent, the pre-emptor may litigate with him and take it from him in the presence of the buyer, just as if the seller were the owner. But if he delivers it to the principal, the agent has no possession or ownership, and he is not a litigant after that, so he becomes like the seller: he is a litigant as long as he has not delivered to the buyer; when he delivers to him, he no longer has possession or ownership, so he leaves being a litigant. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/260) And according to the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i), the litigant is the principal from the beginning, because the agent is like a messenger. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 3/338] And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If someone buys for another without his order, he is a litigant until he delivers the property to the one for whom he bought it. So if the author said: 'And the buyer for another is a litigant until he delivers', it would be more appropriate. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/260) 'And the pre-emptor has the option of sight etc.': meaning the pre-emptor has the option of sight and the option of defect, even if the buyer stipulated exemption from defect, and this is by consensus, because taking by pre-emption is a purchase from the buyer if the taking is after delivery, and if before delivery it is from the seller, so he has the option. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/261) And the option is not voided by the buyer's sight or by his stipulation of exemption, because the pre-emptor is not his representative, so his right is not voided by the buyer's waiver. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 3/338] 'And if they dispute etc.': meaning if the pre-emptor and buyer dispute over the amount of the price, and the pre-emptor says: You bought it for one hundred, and the buyer says: I bought it for one hundred and twenty, the buyer's statement is accepted, because the pre-emptor claims the right to take upon paying the lower amount, and the buyer denies that; the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath, and they do not both swear, because mutual oaths (tahaluf) are established by the text only when denial comes from both sides.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on Kitab al-Shuf'ah from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering several distinct rulings. First, the pre-emptor may also litigate with the seller directly if the property is still in the seller's possession, because the seller has an original right of possession. This is different from a depositary or borrower, who have only derivative possession and thus are not proper litigants. Second, the judge does not hear the pre-emptor's evidence unless the buyer is present, because both the seller and buyer have rights in the property. After the buyer is present, the judge rescinds the sale. If the property has already been delivered to the buyer, the seller's presence is not required. Third, the liability for the price (al-uhdah) – i.e., if the pre-emptor's ownership is later invalidated, who must return the price? According to the Hanafi school, before delivery, the liability is on the seller, because the contract's rights are with the contracting party. The 'three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i) hold that liability is on the buyer, because rights revert to the owner. This is a significant inter-madhhab dispute. Fourth, an agent who buys for a principal is a proper litigant for the pre-emptor as long as the agent has not delivered the property to the principal. After delivery, the principal becomes the litigant. This is because the agent is the actual contractor. The opposing view (the three) holds that the principal is the litigant from the start, as the agent is merely a messenger. Fifth, the pre-emptor has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) and option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), even if the buyer had stipulated exemption from defects. This is by consensus, because the pre-emptor is taking by a new purchase, so he has the same options as any buyer. His options are not affected by the buyer's actions. Sixth, if the pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price, the buyer's word is taken with an oath, because he is the denier of the pre-emptor's claim. They do not both swear (tahaluf) because mutual oaths are only for cases where both parties deny each other's claims.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option to confirm or rescind a contract after seeing the object of sale, if the buyer had not previously seen it.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return the item due to a defect that existed before sale.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oaths taken by both parties when they dispute a contract and both are unable to provide evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price, whose statement is accepted with an oath?
A) The pre-emptor's
B) The buyer's
C) Both must swear (tahaluf)
D) The judge decides arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor has independent options of sight and defect; in price disputes, the buyer's statement with oath prevails, and mutual oaths are not required.

Dispute over Price in Pre-emption and Burden of Proof
Rulings on price disputes and evidence between pre-emptor and purchaser

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default rule for price disputes in shuf'ah, apply the rule when evidence is presented, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on evidence preference, and identify the practical consequence.

Core Idea
In pre-emption disputes over price, the purchaser's statement is taken by default, but when both present evidence, the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred because it is binding.

When a pre-emptor (shafi') seeks to take a property sold to another, the price is often disputed. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq sets out the rules for whose statement is accepted and how evidence is weighed. The Hanafi school uniquely favours the pre-emptor's evidence when both parties bring proof, on the basis of its binding effect. The passage also addresses the scenario where the seller disputes the price with the purchaser, introducing a distinction based on whether the price has been paid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule for price dispute between pre-emptor and purchaser
When no evidence is presented, whose statement is taken? The matn states 'al-qawlu lil-mushtari' (the statement is that of the purchaser). This is because the purchaser is the possessor and the pre-emptor is claiming against him, so the burden of proof is on the pre-emptor.
2
Determine the rule when both parties present formal evidence
If both the shafi' and the mushtari bring bayyinah (two male witnesses) on their claimed prices, the matn says 'fa lil-shafi'i' (then it is for the pre-emptor). However, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred; Abu Yusuf prefers the purchaser's evidence.
The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that the evidences cancel out and the default rule applies.
3
Analyze the scenario when the seller and purchaser dispute the price
The matn distinguishes based on qabd (receipt of the price). If the seller has not received the full price, the pre-emptor takes at what the seller said. If the seller has received the full price, the pre-emptor takes at what the purchaser said. The key condition is full receipt of the price.
If only part of the price has been paid, the seller's statement still governs.
4
Understand the rationale for preferring the pre-emptor's evidence
The Hanafi majority argue that the pre-emptor's evidence is 'mulzimah' (binding) – if accepted, it obligates the purchaser to surrender the property. The purchaser's evidence is not binding; it only gives the pre-emptor a choice to take or leave. Therefore, even though the purchaser's evidence might prove a higher price (stronger in form), the pre-emptor's evidence is stronger in meaning.
This reasoning is used to rebut Abu Yusuf's argument that the purchaser's evidence is preferred for proving a higher price.
5
Apply the rules to a practical example
Consider: A sells to B for 1000. B pays 500. C (shafi') disputes the price with B. A claims he received 1000. Since the price has not been fully paid, C takes at A's stated price (1000). If both C and B bring evidence, C's evidence is preferred per the majority Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها فالقول للمشتري، وإن برهنا فللشفيع، وإن ادعى المشتري ثمنا، وادعى بائعه أقل منه، جزاء أي فالبينة ولم يقبض الثمن، أخذها الشفيع بما قال البائع، وإن قبض أخذها بما قال المشتري، أي والحال أنه أي الدار أي بالثمن الذي قال البائع الثمن الشفيع والدعوى من الجانبين والمشتري لا يدعي على الشفيع شيئا ولا يكون الشفيع منكرا، ولا يكون في معنى ما ورد به النص، فامتنع القياس. رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۸/۲] قال في "التكملة"، وأطلق المؤلف محله، فشمل ما إذا وقع الاختلاف قبل قبض الدار، ونقد الثمن، أو بعدهما قبل التسليم إلى الشفيع أو بعده، لكن في "التاتارخانية": اشترى دارا، وقبضها ونقد الثمن، ثم اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في الثمن، فالقول للمشتري تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦١/٨] فعلم منه أن المسألة مقيدة بما إذا قبض الدار ونقد الثمن، ولذا قال في "الفتح" نقلا عن "الحموي": إن المسألة مقيدة بما إذا كانت الدار ،مقبوضة والثمن منقودا. حبيب الرحمن عفي عنه) وإن برهنا فللشفيع إلخ يعني لو أقام كل واحد من الشفيع والمشتري البينة على دعواه، فالبينة بينة الشفيع عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف البينة بيئة المشتري؛ لأنها تثبت للزيادة والبيئة المثبتة للزيادة أولى، وعند الشافعي وأحمد رحمها تهاترتا، والقول للمشتري، وعنهما يقرع وعند مالك: يحكم بالأعدل وإلا باليمين، ولهما: أن بينة الشفيع أكثر إثباتا ،معنى وإن كانت بينة المشتري أكثر إثباتا صورة؛ لأن البينات للإلزام، وبينة الشفيع ملزمة، بخلاف بينة المشتري، فإن بينة الشفيع إن قبلت وجب على المشتري تسليم الدار إليه وإذا قبلت بينة المشتري لا يجب على الشفيع شيء، بل يتخير بين الأخذ والترك. (ملتقط من العيني والفتح) أقل منه: أي من الثمن الذي ادعاه المشتري. أخذها الشفيع إلخ لأن الأمر إن كان كما قال البائع، فالشفيع يأخذها، وإن كان كما قال المشتري، يكون حطا عن المشتري بدعواه الأقل، وحط البعض يظهر في حق الشفيع كما بينا؛ ولأن تملك المشتري بإيجاب البائع، فكان القول قوله في مقدار الثمن ما دامت مطالبته باقية، فيأخذها الشفيع، ولو كان ما ادعاه البائع أكثر مما ادعاه المشتري، تحالفا، وأيهما نكل ظهر أن الثمن ما يقوله الآخر، فيأخذها الشفيع بذلك، وإن فسخ القاضي العقد بينهما يأخذ الشفيع بما يقوله البائع؛ لأن الفسخ لا يوجب بطلان حق الشفيع؛ ألا ترى أن الدار إذا ردت على البائع بعيب لا يبطل حقه، وإن كان الرد بقضاء. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٤/٨] وإن قبض إلخ أي قبض جميع الثمن، حتى لو قبض بعضه، وبقي منه، فالقول قول البائع كما في "المبسوط"، وإنما يأخذها بما قال المشتري إذا ثبت قبض جميع الثمن بالبينة أو اليمين؛ لأن البائع باستيفائه الثمن خرج من البين، والتحق بالأجانب، فبقي الاختلاف بين الشفيع والمشتري، وقد ثبت أن القول فيه للمشتري. قال في "الشرنبلالية": هذا إذا كان قبض الثمن ظاهرا كما ذكرنا بأن ثبت بالبينة أو اليمين ولو كان غير ظاهر، فقال البائع: بعت الدار بألف وقبضت الثمن، يأخذها الشفيع بألف ولو بدأ بقبض الثمن قبل بيان القدر بأن قال: بعت الدار وقبضت الثمن، وهو ألف درهم لم يلتفت إلى قوله في مقدار الثمن. (فتح)
Book of Pre-emption: 272. Demand for pre-emption and litigation therein. So the statement is that of the purchaser. And if they both present evidence, then it is for the pre-emptor. And if the purchaser claims a price, and his seller claims less than it, and he has not received the price, the pre-emptor takes it at what the seller said. And if he received it, he takes it at what the purchaser said. (Commentary) ... (until the end of the gloss on 'And if he received it' from al-Fath.)
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage opens the section on shuf'ah (pre-emption) litigation. The author states several rules: (1) When the pre-emptor and purchaser dispute the actual sale price, the default rule is that the purchaser's word is taken because he is the possessor and the pre-emptor is claiming a right against him, so the burden is on the pre-emptor. (2) If both bring formal evidence (bayyinah), the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two shaykhs), because the pre-emptor's evidence establishes a right that obligates the purchaser to surrender the property, whereas the purchaser's evidence only would give the pre-emptor a choice. Abu Yusuf differs, favoring the purchaser's evidence because it proves a higher price, which is more beneficial for the purchaser. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that the two evidences cancel out and the purchaser's statement is taken, while Malik uses a different method. (3) When the seller and purchaser dispute the price (the seller claiming higher, the purchaser lower) and the price has not yet been paid, the pre-emptor takes at the seller's stated price, because the seller is the source of title. But if the seller has already received the full price, then the pre-emptor takes at the purchaser's stated price, because the seller is then out of the picture. The commentators restrict these rules to when the property and price have already been exchanged, clarifying that the default rule (purchaser's statement) applies only when the property is in the purchaser's possession and the price fully paid.

Key Terms
شفيع (shafi'): The person entitled to pre-emption (shuf'ah) to acquire the property sold to another.
مشتري (mushtari): The purchaser of the property subject to pre-emption.
بينة (bayyinah): Formal legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.
ثمن (thaman): The sale price of the property.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession; here, receipt of the price by the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both the shafi' and the purchaser present formal evidence regarding the price, whose evidence is preferred according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The purchaser's evidence
B) The shafi''s evidence
C) The two evidences cancel out
D) The seller's evidence

Key Takeaway
In shuf'ah price disputes, the default favours the purchaser, but when both present evidence, the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred; the seller's statement controls before full payment of price.

Effect of Price Reduction and Increase on Pre-emption
Rules on price reduction and increase in shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partial reduction, full reduction, and increase in the sale price, and explain how each affects the pre-emptor's payment.

Core Idea
A partial reduction benefits the pre-emptor; a full reduction voids the sale; an increase does not bind him.

When the seller reduces the price after the sale, does the pre-emptor benefit? The Hanafi school says yes, because the reduction attaches to the contract. But if the seller remits everything, the sale is void. And any extra gift added later does not increase the pre-emptor's liability. This lesson clarifies these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage discusses partial reduction, full reduction, and increase after the sale. Recognize each case and the ruling that applies.
2
Apply the principle for partial reduction
The remission of part is considered part of the original contract; the remaining amount becomes the new price. Thus the pre-emptor pays the reduced amount.
This is the Hanafi view; the three (Zufar, Shafi'i, Malik) disagree.
3
Handle whole remission
If the seller remits the entire price, the sale becomes void (no price), so the pre-emptor's right is extinguished by consensus.
4
Handle increase
Any addition after the contract (e.g., a gift) does not affect the pre-emptor; he pays only the original price, because imposing it would harm him.
Consensus among schools.
5
Consider prior surrender
If the pre-emptor surrendered his right before the reduction, but the reduction occurs later, his right revives. He may take the property at the reduced price.
This is an important practical nuance.

Source Text

وحط البعض يظهر في حق الشفيع، لا حط الكل والزيادة، وإن اشترى دارا البائع أي بعض الثمن أي كل الثمن بعرض أو بعقار أخذها الشفيع بقيمته وبمثله لو مثليا، وبحال لو مؤجلا، أو يصبر أي ما ليس بنقد حتى يمضي الأجل، عن أي الدار المضروب بين البائع والمشتري لأنه أي بقيمة العرض أو العقار عن "الشلبي". أي وأخذها بثمن حال لو كان الثمن مؤجلا وحط البعض إلخ: أي حط بعض الثمن يظهر في حق الشفيع حتى يأخذه بما بقي؛ لأن الحط لما التحق بأصل العقد، صار الباقي هو الثمن، وعند الثلاثة لا يظهر، ولا يسقط عن الشفيع بل عليه الثمن المسمى، وقد مر أصل هذا في كتاب البيوع. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲] ولو علم الشفيع أن المشتري اشتراه بألف، فسلم الشفعة، ثم حط البائع مائة، فله الشفعة كما لو باعه بألف فسلم ثم زاد البائع للمشتري جارية أو متاعا كذا "الدر" "القنية". [رمز الحقائق: ٣٣٩/٢]
And the reduction of part appears in the right of the pre-emptor, not the reduction of the whole nor the increase. And if he bought a house with a commodity or real estate, the pre-emptor takes it at its value, and if it is a fungible (mithli), he takes the like of it. And with a spot price if deferred, or he waits until the term passes. (Commentary...)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct issues in pre-emption (shuf'ah). First, the effect of the seller remitting part of the price after the sale: the pre-emptor benefits from that reduction, so he pays the reduced amount. This is because the remission is considered part of the original contract, so the remaining amount becomes the price. However, if the seller remits the entire price, the sale becomes void (since there is no price), and the pre-emptor's right is extinguished by consensus. Similarly, if the seller later adds something (increase), that does not affect the pre-emptor—he pays only the original price. Second, if the purchaser bought the property with a commodity (like cloth or wheat) or real estate, the pre-emptor must pay the value of that commodity at the time of the original sale (not at the time of taking), or if it is a fungible (mithli), he must give the like of it. Third, if the sale price was deferred (mu'ajjal), the pre-emptor has a choice: he may take the property immediately by paying a spot price (since the deferral was a personal concession to the purchaser, not a property of the debt), or he may wait until the term expires and then take it. Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik disagree, holding that the pre-emptor takes with the same deferred term. The Hanafi reasoning is that deferment is not an inherent quality of the debt but a contractual stipulation that does not transfer to the pre-emptor. The passage also notes that in the case of deferred price, the pre-emptor must still make his demand (talab) immediately; delay without demand invalidates his right according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (and Abu Yusuf's earlier view).

Key Terms
حط (hut): Remission or reduction of the sale price by the seller after the contract.
زيادة (ziyada): An addition or increase given by the seller to the purchaser after the contract (e.g., a gift).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the seller reduces the price after the sale, the pre-emptor must pay:
A) the original price
B) the reduced price
C) half of the reduced price
D) nothing if the reduction is partial

Key Takeaway
A partial reduction of the sale price passes to the pre-emptor; a full remission voids the sale; a unilateral increase does not bind him.

Valuing the Price When the Purchaser Paid with Commodities or Real Estate
Rules on payment when the sale price is a commodity or real estate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine how the pre-emptor must compensate the purchaser when the purchase price was a commodity or real estate, including valuation timing and the rule for fungibles.

Core Idea
When the purchaser paid with goods or land, the pre-emptor pays their value at the time of the original sale, not at the time of taking.

Sometimes the purchaser buys the property not with cash but with a commodity (like cloth or wheat) or another piece of real estate. How does the pre-emptor pay? The Hanafi school rules that he pays the value of that commodity at the time of the original transaction, or gives the same quantity if it is a fungible. This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Determine the nature of the price
Identify whether the purchaser paid with a commodity (e.g., goods, animals) or real estate (land). This affects how the pre-emptor compensates.
2
Check if it is a fungible (mithli) or non-fungible (qimi)
A fungible item (like wheat, oil) can be replaced by the same type and measure. A non-fungible (like a specific cow or a piece of land) must be valued.
Mithli defined by similar items in the market.
3
Apply the valuation rule
For non-fungible: pre-emptor pays the market value of the commodity at the time of the original sale. For fungible: he gives the like (same type and quantity).
4
Note the timing of valuation
The value is determined at the time the purchaser bought the property, not at the time the pre-emptor takes it.
This is crucial because values may fluctuate.

Deferred Payment and the Pre-emptor's Right to Pay Spot
Rules on deferred payment and demand in pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that the pre-emptor may pay spot immediately or wait, and evaluate the dispute with Zufar and Shafi'i, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on demand timing.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor is not bound by the purchaser's deferred payment term; he may pay spot or wait, but he must demand immediately.

When the purchase price is deferred (paid later), does the pre-emptor have to wait? The Hanafi school gives him a choice: pay now with spot cash, or wait until the term expires. But he cannot take the property with a deferred payment. This lesson delves into the reasoning and the disagreements, including a notable intra-Hanafi difference on whether immediate demand is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the pre-emptor's two options
The pre-emptor may either pay spot price immediately and take the property, or wait until the deferred term expires and then take it.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
Deferment is a stipulation (shart) benefiting the purchaser, not an inherent quality of the debt. It does not transfer to the pre-emptor because it is personal.
Evidence: if the purchaser resells with deferred payment, the deferment does not automatically pass.
3
Compare with opposing school's reasoning
Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik argue that the pre-emptor takes the property exactly as the purchaser took it, including the deferred term.
They view deferment as an attribute of the debt.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on demand timing
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say demand must be made immediately upon knowledge, even if the price is deferred; delay invalidates. Abu Yusuf later retracted this view.
The mufta bihi is immediate demand.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The reliable position: pre-emptor may pay spot or wait; demand must be immediate.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲] ولو علم الشفيع أن المشتري اشتراه بألف، فسلم الشفعة، ثم حط البائع مائة، فله الشفعة كما لو باعه بألف فسلم ثم زاد البائع للمشتري جارية أو متاعا كذا "الدر" "القنية". (عيني، فتح) لا حط الكل إلخ : يعني إذا حط البائع كل الثمن عن المشتري لم يسقط عن الشفيع بالإجماع؛ يصير بيعا بلا ثمن، وأنه باطل. (عيني) والزيادة إلخ: أي ولا يظهر الزيادة على الثمن بعد عقد البيع في حق الشفيع حتى لا تلزمه الزيادة بالإجماع؛ لأن في اعتبارها إلحاق الضرر به، وهو مدفوع رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲] وإن اشترى دارا إلخ: يعني إذا اشترى المشتري دارا بعرض أو عقار ، أخذها الشفيع بقيمة ذلك العرض أو العقار، وأخذها بمثل ذلك العرض لو كان مثليا أي من ذوات الأمثال كالمكيل والموزون والعددي المتقارب كما في ضمان العدوان رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲] وتعتبر قيمته وقت الشراء لا وقت الأخذ كذا في "الفتح" نقلا . لو مؤجلا يعني يأخذها الشفيع الدار من المشتري بثمن حال إذا كان الثمن مؤجلا أو يصبر حتى يمضي الأجل، فيأخذها عند ذلك، وليس له أن يأخذها في الحال بثمن مؤجل، وقال زفر والشافعي ومالك: له ذلك؛ لأنه يأخذ بمثل ما أخذ المشتري بصفته، والأجل صفة الدين ولنا أن الأجل يثبت بالشرط، وليس من لوازم العقد، فاشتراطه في حق المشتري لا يكون اشتراطا في حق الشفيع؛ لتفاوت الناس فيه؛ ولأن الأجل حق المطلوب، والدين حق الطالب، ولهذا لو باع المشتري ما اشتراه بثمن مؤجل مرابحة أو تولية لا يثبت الأجل من غير شرط، ولو كان صفة له لثبت، ثم إن من البائع بثمن حال، سقط الثمن من المشتري؛ لتحول الصفقة إلى الشفيع على ما بينا، ورجع البائع على المشتري بثمن مؤجل، وإن اختار الانتظار كان له ذلك تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٦/٨] أو يصبر إلخ: أي يصبر الشفيع عن الأخذ إلى حلول الأجل وأما الطلب فلا بد منه في الحال، حتى لو سكت و لم يطلب بطلت شفعته عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وبه كان يقول أبو يوسف أولا، ثم رجع عنه، وقال: لا تبطل شفعته بالتأخير إلى حلول الأجل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٧/٨] لأن الطلب ليس مقصود لذاته، بل للأخذ، وهو لا يتمكن منه في الحال بثمن مؤجل، فلا فائدة بطلبه في الحال ولهما أن حقه قد ثبت ولهذا له أن يأخذه بثمن حال، ولو لا أن حقه ثابت لما كان له ذلك، والسكوت عن الطلب بعد ثبوت حقه يبطل الشفعة. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٣٩/٢]
And the reduction of part appears in the right of the pre-emptor, not the reduction of the whole nor the increase. And if he bought a house with a commodity or real estate, the pre-emptor takes it at its value, and if it is a fungible (mithli), he takes the like of it. And with a spot price if deferred, or he waits until the term passes. (Commentary...)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct issues in pre-emption (shuf'ah). First, the effect of the seller remitting part of the price after the sale: the pre-emptor benefits from that reduction, so he pays the reduced amount. This is because the remission is considered part of the original contract, so the remaining amount becomes the price. However, if the seller remits the entire price, the sale becomes void (since there is no price), and the pre-emptor's right is extinguished by consensus. Similarly, if the seller later adds something (increase), that does not affect the pre-emptor—he pays only the original price. Second, if the purchaser bought the property with a commodity (like cloth or wheat) or real estate, the pre-emptor must pay the value of that commodity at the time of the original sale (not at the time of taking), or if it is a fungible (mithli), he must give the like of it. Third, if the sale price was deferred (mu'ajjal), the pre-emptor has a choice: he may take the property immediately by paying a spot price (since the deferral was a personal concession to the purchaser, not a property of the debt), or he may wait until the term expires and then take it. Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik disagree, holding that the pre-emptor takes with the same deferred term. The Hanafi reasoning is that deferment is not an inherent quality of the debt but a contractual stipulation that does not transfer to the pre-emptor. The passage also notes that in the case of deferred price, the pre-emptor must still make his demand (talab) immediately; delay without demand invalidates his right according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (and Abu Yusuf's earlier view).

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The deferred term for payment of the price.
طلب (talab): The formal demand made by the pre-emptor to assert his right, which must be performed immediately upon knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the sale price is deferred, the pre-emptor:
A) must wait until the term expires
B) may take immediately with a spot price
C) must take with the same deferred term
D) loses his right if he does not wait

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor may pay spot and take immediately or wait, but he must demand immediately upon knowledge.

Effect of Price Reduction and Increase on the Pre-emptor
Explains three rulings on post-sale price changes in shuf'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partial reduction, full reduction, and increase in the sale price after a contract, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on partial reduction.

Core Idea
A partial price reduction benefits the pre-emptor; a full reduction or increase does not.

In shuf'a (pre-emption), the right to take the property at the sale price is well-established. But what happens if the seller and buyer modify the price after the contract? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies three scenarios: partial reduction, full reduction, and an increase (e.g., a gift). The rulings involve both consensus and a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement. Understanding these nuances ensures the pre-emptor pays the correct amount and is not unfairly burdened.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three post-sale modifications
The passage discusses three types of changes to the sale price after the contract: partial reduction (hatt al-ba'd), full reduction (hatt al-kull), and an increase (ziyada) such as a gift.
2
State the effect of each on the pre-emptor
For each modification, determine whether the pre-emptor benefits or not according to the matn and the commentary.
Partial reduction benefits the pre-emptor (Abu Hanifa's view); full reduction does not; increase does not.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on partial reduction
The passage notes that according to the 'three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar), a partial reduction does not benefit the pre-emptor; he must pay the original named price. Abu Hanifa holds the opposite view.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's.
4
Understand the legal reasoning behind the dispute
Abu Hanifa views the reduction as attaching to the original contract, making the remaining amount the effective price. The three see it as a separate new transaction.
The reasoning is based on the nature of the reduction: does it integrate into the original contract or stand alone?
5
Distinguish between total reduction and increase
For full reduction, there is consensus that the pre-emptor is not relieved of the original price because the sale would be without a price and void. For an increase, consensus that the pre-emptor does not owe extra, to avoid harm.
These rulings are agreed upon, unlike partial reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحط البعض يظهر في حق الشفيع، لا حط الكل والزيادة. وحط البعض إلخ: أي حط بعض الثمن يظهر في حق الشفيع حتى يأخذه بما بقي؛ لأن الحط لما التحق بأصل العقد، صار الباقي هو الثمن، وعند الثلاثة لا يظهر، ولا يسقط عن الشفيع بل عليه الثمن المسمى، وقد مر أصل هذا في كتاب البيوع. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲] ولو علم الشفيع أن المشتري اشتراه بألف، فسلم الشفعة، ثم حط البائع مائة، فله الشفعة كما لو باعه بألف فسلم ثم زاد البائع للمشتري جارية أو متاعا كذا "الدر" "القنية". (عيني، فتح) لا حط الكل إلخ : يعني إذا حط البائع كل الثمن عن المشتري لم يسقط عن الشفيع بالإجماع؛ يصير بيعا بلا ثمن، وأنه باطل. (عيني) والزيادة إلخ: أي ولا يظهر الزيادة على الثمن بعد عقد البيع في حق الشفيع حتى لا تلزمه الزيادة بالإجماع؛ لأن في اعتبارها إلحاق الضرر به، وهو مدفوع رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲]
And the reduction of part (of the price) becomes apparent in favor of the pre-emptor, not the reduction of the whole and the increase. And 'reduction of part' etc.: i.e., the reduction of part of the price becomes apparent in favor of the pre-emptor, so that he takes it at what remains; because when the reduction is attached to the original contract, what remains becomes the price. According to the three (imams), it does not become apparent, and it is not dropped from the pre-emptor, rather he owes the named price, and the origin of this has passed in the Book of Sales. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/339] And if the pre-emptor knew that the buyer bought it for a thousand and he surrendered the shuf'a, then the seller reduced one hundred, he has the shuf'a, just as if he sold it for a thousand and he surrendered, then the seller gave the buyer a slave-girl or goods etc. (al-Durr, al-Qunyah) ('Ayni, Fath) 'Not the reduction of the whole' etc.: meaning if the seller reduces the entire price from the buyer, it is not dropped from the pre-emptor by consensus; it becomes a sale without price, which is void. ('Ayni) 'And the increase' etc.: i.e., the increase upon the price after the sales contract does not become apparent in favor of the pre-emptor, so that the increase is not binding on him by consensus; because considering it would cause harm to him, and harm is repelled. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/339)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation on how post-contract price modifications affect the pre-emptor. The matn (from Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states three rules: (1) a partial reduction of the price benefits the pre-emptor—he pays the reduced amount; (2) a full reduction does not benefit him—he still pays the original price because the sale becomes void; (3) an increase (extra gift) does not benefit him—he does not owe extra. The commentator (probably from al-'Inayah or Fath al-Qadir) explains that for partial reduction, since it attaches to the original contract, the remaining amount becomes the effective price. However, according to the 'three' (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar), the reduction does not benefit the pre-emptor; he must pay the original named price. The proof given is that the reduction is like a new transaction. For full reduction, there is consensus that the pre-emptor still owes the full price because a sale without a price is void. For increase, consensus also that the pre-emptor does not owe extra, because imposing it would harm him, and harm is prevented in Islamic law.

Key Terms
حط البعض (hatt al-ba'd): Reduction of part of the sale price by the seller after the contract; in shuf'a, it benefits the pre-emptor according to Abu Hanifa.
لا حط الكل (lā hatt al-kull): Reduction of the entire price; does not affect the pre-emptor because the sale becomes void without a price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if the seller reduces part of the price after the sale, what is the effect on the pre-emptor?
A) The pre-emptor pays the original named price, unaffected by the reduction.
B) The pre-emptor pays the reduced price, benefiting from the reduction.
C) The pre-emptor must pay the entire reduced amount plus an extra penalty.
D) The pre-emptor loses his right to shuf'a entirely.

Key Takeaway
In shuf'a, post-sale partial price reduction benefits the pre-emptor only according to Abu Hanifa; total reduction and increase do not affect his obligation by consensus.

Determining the Pre-emptor's Payment When Buyer Paid with Goods or Real Estate
Ruling on pre-emptor's payment for non-cash purchases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for the pre-emptor's payment when the buyer paid with non-cash assets, and apply it to distinguish between mithli and qimi assets and the correct valuation time.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor pays the like of a fungible (mithli) commodity or the value of a non-fungible (qimi) asset, valued at the time of the original purchase.

When a property is bought with something other than cash—like goods or real estate—the pre-emptor cannot simply pay the current market price of the house. Instead, Islamic law looks to the asset the buyer actually gave. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies how to calculate the pre-emptor's payment: if the asset is fungible (mithli), the pre-emptor must deliver the same quantity and quality; if non-fungible, he pays its cash value at the time of the original sale. Understanding this prevents disputes over fluctuating prices and ensures justice between buyer and pre-emptor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original payment asset
Determine what the buyer actually gave: was it a commodity (ard) or real estate (aqar)? If it is a commodity, classify it as mithli (fungible) or qimi (non-fungible). Mithli items are those that have standard exact equivalents: measured, weighed, or uniform countable goods like wheat, barley, eggs, or money.
Real estate is always qimi (unique).
2
Apply the rule for mithli vs. qimi
If the payment was mithli, the pre-emptor must give the exact like: same quantity and quality of that commodity. If qimi, he pays the cash value of that commodity or real estate at the time of the original purchase—not at the time he takes the property.
This mirrors the rule in wrongful guarantee (daman al-'udwan).
3
Handle deferred payment
If the original price was deferred (muajjal), the pre-emptor may either pay cash immediately or wait until the term expires and then pay the deferred amount.
This is mentioned in the main text: 'in cash if deferred, or wait until the term expires.'
4
Verify the valuation time
Confirm that the valuation is at the time of the original purchase, not at the time of taking. This prevents the pre-emptor from profiting from price increases or suffering from decreases.
Al-Fath quotes this as the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى دارا بعرض أو بعقار أخذها الشفيع بقيمته وبمثله لو مثليا، وبحال لو مؤجلا، أو يصبر حتى يمضي الأجل، عن أي بقيمة العرض أو العقار عن أي وأخذها بثمن حال لو كان الثمن مؤجلا. وإن اشترى دارا إلخ: يعني إذا اشترى المشتري دارا بعرض أو عقار، أخذها الشفيع بقيمة ذلك العرض أو العقار، وأخذها بمثل ذلك العرض لو كان مثليا أي من ذوات الأمثال كالمكيل والموزون والعددي المتقارب كما في ضمان العدوان رمز الحقائق: ۳۳۹/۲] وتعتبر قيمته وقت الشراء لا وقت الأخذ كذا في "الفتح" نقلا.
And if he bought a house with a commodity or with real estate, the pre-emptor takes it at its value, and by its like if it is mithli, and in cash if it is deferred, or he waits until the term expires. (The phrase) 'by its value of the commodity or real estate' (means): and he takes it with a cash price if the price was deferred. 'And if he bought a house' etc.: meaning if the buyer bought a house with a commodity or real estate, the pre-emptor takes it at the value of that commodity or real estate, and he takes it by the like of that commodity if it is mithli, i.e., from things that have mithliyat, like measured, weighed, and countable similar items as in wrongful guarantee. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/339) And its value is considered at the time of purchase, not at the time of taking; likewise in al-Fath, quoted.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how to determine the price a pre-emptor must pay when the original buyer paid with non-monetary assets (either a commodity like goods or real estate). The matn states: if the buyer purchased the property with a commodity or real estate, the pre-emptor takes it at the value of that commodity or real estate. The commentator explains further: if the commodity is of the type that has an exact equivalent (mithli), like measured goods (kili) or weighed goods (wazn) or closely similar counted items, then the pre-emptor must give an identical commodity, not its value. This is analogous to the rule in wrongful guarantee (daman al-'udwan). The value is assessed at the time of the original purchase, not at the time the pre-emptor takes possession. This is important because prices may fluctuate. The source of this ruling is Ramz al-Haqa'iq and the commentary of al-Fath.

Key Terms
مثلي (mithlī): A commodity that has a standard like (mithl) that can be exactly matched, e.g., wheat, eggs, money.
ذوات الأمثال (dhawāt al-amthāl): Things that have equivalents: measured, weighed, or uniform countable items.
قيمة (qīmah): Cash value of a non-mithli asset at the time of the original purchase.
عرض (‘araḍ): A commodity, i.e., a movable good other than money or real estate.
عقار (‘iqār): Real estate, immovable property like land or buildings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house was purchased with 200 litres of olive oil (a mithli commodity), what must the pre-emptor give to take the house?
A) The current cash value of 200 litres of olive oil.
B) 200 litres of olive oil of the same quality.
C) The value of the house at the time of purchase.
D) Any amount of olive oil as long as it is the same quality.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor must pay the same fungible goods or the cash value of the original payment at the time of purchase, preserving the transaction's fairness.

Pre-emption and Deferred Payment: Can the Pre-emptor Inherit the Term?
Ruling when the buyer's payment was deferred; pre-emptor's options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-emption when payment is deferred, identify the opposing positions, and explain the Hanafi argument that the term is a personal stipulation, not a debt attribute.

Core Idea
A deferred payment term does not transfer to the pre-emptor because it is a personal stipulation, not a debt attribute.

When a property is sold with deferred payment, the pre-emptor has the right to take it. But does he inherit the same payment term? The Hanafi school says no, he must pay cash or wait, while other schools allow him to take with the same deferred term. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this difference.

Guided Path
1
1. State the Hanafi position
The pre-emptor can either pay cash immediately or wait until the term expires; he cannot take the property with a deferred price.
Two options, no third.
2
2. Identify the opposing view
Zufar (a Hanafi scholar), al-Shafi'i, and Malik all say the pre-emptor may take with the same deferred term.
They consider the term an attribute of the debt.
3
3. Follow the opposing proof
The opponent argues: the pre-emptor takes the like of what the buyer took with its attributes; the term is an attribute of the debt, so it transfers.
Attribute (sifah) is a quality inherent in the thing.
4
4. Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the term is established by conditional agreement (shart), not a necessary attribute. People differ in creditworthiness, so it does not automatically transfer. Evidence: if the buyer resells without mentioning the term, the term is not established.
Analogy (qiyas) from murabahah/tawliyah sale.
5
5. Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, the pre-emptor cannot take with deferred payment; under opposing views, he can. This affects the financial planning of the pre-emptor and the seller.
Also affects the seller's recourse against the buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو مؤجلا يعني يأخذها الشفيع الدار من المشتري بثمن حال إذا كان الثمن مؤجلا أو يصبر حتى يمضي الأجل، فيأخذها عند ذلك، وليس له أن يأخذها في الحال بثمن مؤجل، وقال زفر والشافعي ومالك: له ذلك؛ لأنه يأخذ بمثل ما أخذ المشتري بصفته، والأجل صفة الدين، ولنا أن الأجل يثبت بالشرط، وليس من لوازم العقد، فاشتراطه في حق المشتري لا يكون اشتراطا في حق الشفيع؛ لتفاوت الناس فيه؛ ولأن الأجل حق المطلوب، والدين حق الطالب، ولهذا لو باع المشتري ما اشتراه بثمن مؤجل مرابحة أو تولية لا يثبت الأجل من غير شرط، ولو كان صفة له لثبت، ثم إن من البائع بثمن حال، سقط الثمن من المشتري؛ لتحول الصفقة إلى الشفيع على ما بينا، ورجع البائع على المشتري بثمن مؤجل، وإن اختار الانتظار كان له ذلك تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٦/٨]
If it is deferred: meaning the pre-emptor takes the house from the buyer with a cash price if the price was deferred, or he waits until the term expires, then takes it at that point, and he does not have the right to take it immediately with a deferred price. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik said: he has that (right); because he takes the like of what the buyer took with its attribute, and the term is an attribute of the debt. Our (Hanafi) proof is that the term is established by stipulation, and is not a necessary concomitant of the contract; so stipulating it in favor of the buyer does not become a stipulation in favor of the pre-emptor, because people differ in this; and because the term is a right of the debtor (the buyer), and the debt is a right of the creditor (the seller). For this reason, if the buyer sold what he bought with a deferred price as murabahah or tawliyah, the term is not established without a stipulation, and if it were an attribute, it would be established. Then, if he (the pre-emptor) takes from the seller with a cash price, the price from the buyer falls due to the transfer of the sale to the pre-emptor as we have explained, and the seller has recourse against the buyer for the deferred price. And if he (the pre-emptor) chooses to wait, that is permissible for him. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/266)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the pre-emptor's right when the buyer purchased with a deferred payment. The matn says the pre-emptor can take with cash or wait, but cannot take with a deferred payment. Zufar (a Hanafi imam), al-Shafi'i, and Malik disagree: they say the pre-emptor may take with the same deferred term because the term is an attribute of the debt, and the pre-emptor steps into the buyer's shoes. The Hanafi response argues that the term is a stipulation (shart) specific to the buyer, not an essential attribute of the debt. People differ in their creditworthiness, so the term does not automatically transfer. They support this with an analogy: if the buyer resells the property on the same deferred term without stipulation, the term does not carry over. Therefore, the pre-emptor may only take immediately with cash (in which case the seller returns the deferred price to the buyer) or wait for the original term to expire.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): A deferred term for payment; legally, a time period after which payment is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the buyer purchased the property on a one-year deferred payment, what are the pre-emptor's options?
A) Take the property immediately by paying the same one-year deferred price.
B) Take the property immediately with cash or wait one year to take without cash payment.
C) Cancel the pre-emption right entirely.
D) Take the property immediately with a new deferred agreement with the seller.

Key Takeaway
A deferred payment term is a personal stipulation for the buyer; it does not transfer to the pre-emptor, who must pay cash or wait.

intermediate
8 min
Immediate Demand in Pre-emption with Deferred Price
Intra-Hanafi dispute on talab timing when price is deferred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf's later position on the need for immediate demand (talab) in shuf'a when the price is deferred, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Silence after knowledge forfeits shuf'a — even if the price is deferred, demand must be immediate.

In pre-emption (shuf'a), the right must be asserted by a formal demand (talab) immediately upon learning of the sale. But what if the price is deferred and the pre-emptor cannot take the property until later? Does he still have to demand immediately, or can he wait? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on this exact question, contrasting the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) with Abu Yusuf's later retraction.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the majority view
Identify the contested ruling: whether immediate demand is required when the price is deferred. State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: immediate demand is necessary; silence forfeits the right.
The majority view is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially agreed, then retracted and said the pre-emptor may delay demand until the term expires without losing his right.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the later opinion is weaker in authority.
3
Analyze the proofs of both sides
Abu Yusuf's reasoning: demand is only for taking, and taking is impossible immediately with a deferred price, so demand serves no purpose. The majority's proof: the right is established at the moment of sale, and the pre-emptor can take with cash immediately; silence after right is established nullifies it.
The majority's proof rests on the principle that a right once established must be asserted or it is lost.
4
Evaluate the tarjih and state the mufta bihi
The text notes that Abu Yusuf's earlier opinion was the same as the majority, and his later retraction is not preferred. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: immediate demand required.
This tarjih is based on zahir al-riwayah and the majority of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الطلب فلا بد منه في الحال، حتى لو سكت و لم يطلب بطلت شفعته عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وبه كان يقول أبو يوسف أولا، ثم رجع عنه، وقال: لا تبطل شفعته بالتأخير إلى حلول الأجل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٧/٨] لأن الطلب ليس مقصود لذاته، بل للأخذ، وهو لا يتمكن منه في الحال بثمن مؤجل، فلا فائدة بطلبه في الحال ولهما أن حقه قد ثبت ولهذا له أن يأخذه بثمن حال، ولو لا أن حقه ثابت لما كان له ذلك، والسكوت عن الطلب بعد ثبوت حقه يبطل الشفعة. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٣٩/٢]
And as for the demand (talab), it is necessary immediately, such that if he remains silent and does not demand, his shuf'a is nullified according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf used to say that initially, but then he retracted and said: his shuf'a is not nullified by delay until the term expires. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/267) Because the demand is not intended for itself, but for taking, and he is not able to take immediately with a deferred price, so there is no benefit in demanding immediately. But the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that his right has been established, and therefore he can take it with a cash price; if his right were not established, he would not have that. And silence after the establishment of his right nullifies the shuf'a. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/339)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the pre-emptor must make an immediate demand (talab) when the price is deferred. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the pre-emptor must demand immediately upon knowledge of the sale; if he remains silent without demanding, his shuf'a is forfeited, regardless of the deferred term. Abu Yusuf initially agreed, but later retracted and argued that since the pre-emptor cannot take the property immediately with a deferred price (he can only take with cash or wait), imposing an immediate demand has no benefit; therefore, his right should not be lost by delaying demand until the term expires. The proof of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that the pre-emptor's right is established from the moment of the sale, and he can take with cash; since he has the option to take immediately, silence negates the right. This ikhtilaf is noted in the commentary from Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Ramz al-Haqa'iq.

Key Terms
طلب (talab): The formal demand for pre-emption made by the pre-emptor to establish his right; must be immediate upon knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if the pre-emptor learns of the sale but remains silent because the price is deferred, what happens to his shuf'a?
A) His shuf'a remains valid until the term expires.
B) His shuf'a is forfeited because he did not demand immediately.
C) His shuf'a is automatically extended for the duration of the term.
D) He can demand at any time before the term expires without losing the right.

Key Takeaway
Immediate demand (talab) is required upon knowledge of the sale, even if the price is deferred; silence forfeits the right of pre-emption according to the mufta bihi opinion of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Pre-emption When Property Was Bought with Wine or Pig
Ruling on pre-emption when property was bought with wine or pig.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'a when the sale consideration includes wine or pig, and apply the correct payment method based on the pre-emptor's religion.

Core Idea
Shuf'a applies even when sale is for wine or pig among Dhimmis; payment depends on pre-emptor's religion.

In Hanafi fiqh, Dhimmis are permitted to transact in wine and pork. This lesson examines how the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) operates when the underlying sale was made with these prohibited items. You will learn the ruling for a Dhimmi pre-emptor, a Muslim pre-emptor, and mixed or converted scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and parties
A Dhimmi buys property from another Dhimmi paying with wine or a pig. This sale is valid among Dhimmis. Determine the religion of the pre-emptor.
2
Apply the payment rule for Dhimmi pre-emptor
If the pre-emptor is Dhimmi, he takes the property by delivering the like (mithl) of wine (since wine is mithli among them) and the value (qima) of the pig (since pig is qimi).
The original buyer must accept this; shuf'a is a right.
3
Apply the payment rule for Muslim pre-emptor
If the pre-emptor is Muslim, he cannot take possession of wine or pig; therefore, he pays the market value of both.
4
Handle mixed or converted pre-emptors
If there are two pre-emptors, one Muslim and one Dhimmi, each takes half: Muslim pays half the value, Dhimmi pays half the like/value accordingly. If a Dhimmi pre-emptor converts to Islam before taking, his ruling becomes that of a Muslim.
5
Note the apostate exception
An apostate (murtadd) has no right to shuf'a, nor do his heirs, because shuf'a is not inherited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير إن كان الشفيع ذميا، وبقيمتهما لو مسلما، ويأخذ بقيمة الخمر والخنزير كان الشفيع. فيأخذها: أي الدار بعد مضي الأجل. وبمثل الخمر إلخ : أي أخذها الشفيع بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير إن كان الشفيع ذميا، صورته: اشترى ذمي من ذمي عقارا بخمر أو خنزير فإن كان شفيعه ذميا، أخذه بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير؛ لأن هذا فيما بينهم، فإذا صح ترتب عليه أحكام البيع مثل الشفعة ونحوها غير أن الذمي لا يتعذر صحيح عليه تسليم الخمر، فيأخذه بها؛ لأنها من ذوات الأمثال والخنزير من ذوات القيم، فيجب عليه قيمته. [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٠/٢] ولو كان الشفيع مرتدا لا شفعة له، سواء كان قتل على ردته أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب، ولا لورثته؛ لأن الشفعة لا تورث كذا في "العناية". (فتح) وبقيمتهما لو مسلما أي أخذ الدار بقيمة الخمر وقيمة الخنزير لو كان الشفيع مسلما؛ لأنه لا يقدر على تسليم المثل؛ لكونه ممنوعا عن تمليكهما، فيجب عليه قيمتهما كما في ضمان العدوان، وإن كان الشفيع مسلما وذميا، أخذ كل واحد منهما النصف بما ذكرنا من قيمة الخمر أو مثلها، ولو أسلم الذمي صار حكمه حكم المسلم من الابتداء، فيأخذها بالقيمة، والمستأمن كالذمي في جميع ذلك. عيني بتصرف من المحشي)
And by the like of wine and the value of a pig if the pre-emptor is a Dhimmi, and by their value if he is Muslim, and he takes by the value of wine and pig if the pre-emptor was. 'He takes it': i.e., the house after the term expires. 'And by the like of wine' etc.: i.e., the pre-emptor takes by the like of wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is a Dhimmi. Its example: a Dhimmi bought a property from a Dhimmi with wine or a pig; if his pre-emptor is a Dhimmi, he takes it by the like of wine and the value of the pig; because this is among them (it is valid between them), so if it is valid, the rulings of sale follow, such as shuf'a and similar, except that the Dhimmi is not prevented from delivering wine, so he takes it with it; because it is from among things with likes (mithliyat) and the pig is from among things of value (qimiyat), so its value is obligatory. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/340] And if the pre-emptor is an apostate, he has no shuf'a, whether he was killed for apostasy or died or joined the abode of war, nor do his heirs; because shuf'a is not inherited. Likewise in al-'Inayah. (Fath) 'And by their value if Muslim': i.e., he takes the house by the value of the wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is Muslim; because he is not able to deliver the like, being prohibited from making them owned, so he must pay their value as in wrongful guarantee. And if the pre-emptor consists of a Muslim and a Dhimmi, each of them takes half according to what we mentioned of the value of wine or its like. And if the Dhimmi converts to Islam, his ruling becomes the ruling of a Muslim from the beginning, so he takes it by value. And a musta'min is like a Dhimmi in all of that. ('Ayni, with adaptation from the hashiyah)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on pre-emption when the underlying sale involved wine or a pig, which are invalid for Muslims but valid between Dhimmis. If both buyer and pre-emptor are Dhimmis, the pre-emptor takes the property by delivering the like of wine (since wine is mithli among Dhimmis) and the value of the pig (since pig is qimi). If the pre-emptor is Muslim, he cannot take delivery of wine or pig because that is prohibited in Islam; therefore, he must pay their value instead. If there are two pre-emptors, one Muslim and one Dhimmi, each takes half of the property: the Muslim pays half the value, the Dhimmi pays half the like/value according to his status. If a Dhimmi pre-emptor converts to Islam before taking, he is treated as Muslim and must pay value. A musta'min (non-Muslim with aman) is like a Dhimmi in these rulings. Additionally, an apostate pre-emptor has no right to shuf'a, and his right does not pass to heirs.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who holds a covenant (dhimma).
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate from Islam; loses right to shuf'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Dhimmi buys property with wine and the pre-emptor is a Muslim, how does the Muslim pre-emptor take the property?
A) He takes it by delivering the same wine to the buyer.
B) He takes it by paying the value of the wine.
C) He cannot take the property because wine is prohibited.
D) He waits until the buyer converts to Islam.

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'a attaches to valid sales even if consideration is wine or pig; the pre-emptor's religion dictates the mode of payment.

Pre-emptor's Options When Buyer Built or Planted
Ruling on pre-emptor's options when buyer improved land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi relied-upon position and the opposing views regarding the pre-emptor's options when the buyer has built or planted on the land, and apply the underlying qiyas analogy.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor can force removal of improvements; the buyer's acts are revocable because the pre-emptor's right is superior.

This passage addresses a key practical question in shuf'a (pre-emption): what happens when the buyer has built or planted on the land before the pre-emptor exercises his right? The Hanafi school gives the pre-emptor two starkly different options, but scholars differ on whether he can force the buyer to remove the improvements. Let us explore the reasoning behind each view and understand which position is operative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two regimes
The passage offers two contrasting sets of options: one from the relied-upon Hanafi view (zahir al-riwayah) and another from Abu Yusuf (shared by al-Shafi'i and Malik). First, list what each regime permits the pre-emptor to do.
Focus on the pre-emptor's right to force removal vs. pay for improvements.
2
Examine the opposing school's proof
Abu Yusuf and others argue the buyer is not a wrongdoer since he owned by valid purchase. Their analogy compares the buyer to a donee or buyer in a fasid sale, who built with the owner's permission.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes the buyer here from one who built with permission. Key analogy: a mortgagor who builds on mortgaged property – his building is revocable because the mortgagee has a prior right. Similarly, the pre-emptor's right attaches from the moment of sale, so the buyer's improvements are subject to that right.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the relied-upon view, the pre-emptor can choose to force removal or pay; under Abu Yusuf's view, he cannot force removal – he must either pay for improvements or abandon shuf'a. Note how this affects transaction costs and bargaining power.
5
Apply the relied-upon position
State the mufta bihi: the pre-emptor may force removal. Understand that all buyer's dispositions (including waqf, mosque, cemetery) are revocable due to the strength of the shuf'a right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس لو بنى المشتري أو غرس، أو كلف المشتري قلعهما، وأخذ الشفيع. وبالثمن إلخ : يعني إذا بنى المشتري في الأرض المشفوعة، ثم قضي للشفيع بالشفعة، فالشفيع بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعا، وإن شاء كلف المشتري قلعهما، فيأخذ الأرض فارغة، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يكلف بالقلع، ولكنه بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس، وإن شاء ترك، وبه قال الشافعي ومالك؛ لأنه ليس متعديا في البناء والغرس؛ لثبوت ملكه فيه بالشراء، فلا يعامل بأحكام العدوان، فصار كالموهوب له والمشتري شراء فاسدا عند الإمام، ووجه ظاهر الرواية : أنه بنى في محل تعلق به حق متأكد لغيره من غير تسليط منه فينتقض كالراهن إذا بنى في المرهون ولهذا تنتقض جميع تصرفات المشتري حتى الوقف والمسجد والمقبرة بخلاف الموهوب على قول أبي حنيفة والمشتري شراء فاسدا؛ لأنه فعل بتسليط المالك، من ولهذا لا ينتقض تصرفهما تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٦٨/٨]
And by the price and the value of the building and the planting if the buyer built or planted, or he (the pre-emptor) obliges the buyer to remove them, and the pre-emptor takes. 'And by the price' etc.: meaning if the buyer built or planted on the land subject to shuf'a, then shuf'a is decreed for the pre-emptor, then the pre-emptor has the option: if he wishes, he takes the land by paying the price and the value of the building and planting when removed; if he wishes, he obliges the buyer to remove them, and he takes the land empty. According to Abu Yusuf: he (the pre-emptor) is not authorized to compel removal, but he has the option: if he wishes, he takes by the price and value of building and planting (without removal); if he wishes, he leaves (i.e., abandons shuf'a). And this is also the view of al-Shafi'i and Malik; because the buyer was not a transgressor in building and planting, as his ownership was established by purchase, so he is not treated under rules of wrongful acts. Thus, he becomes like a donee or a buyer in a fasid (defective) purchase according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa?). The reason of the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) is that he built on a place where a strong right of another was attached, without his permission, so it is revocable, like the case of a mortgagor who builds on the mortgaged property. For this reason, all the buyer's dispositions are revocable, even if they be waqf (endowment), mosque, or cemetery, unlike the donee according to Abu Hanifa and the buyer in a fasid purchase; because he acted with the permission of the owner, therefore their dispositions are not revocable. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/368)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute (also intra-Hanafi) regarding the pre-emptor's options when the buyer has made improvements (building or planting) on the land before the shuf'a is taken. The matn gives two clear options: (1) pay the price and the value of the improvements (as removed) and take the land, or (2) force the buyer to remove the improvements and take the land empty. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he says the pre-emptor cannot force removal; instead he can either pay for the improvements (without removal) or abandon the shuf'a. Al-Shafi'i and Malik hold the same view as Abu Yusuf. Their reasoning is that the buyer is not a wrongdoer (ghāsb); he bought legally and built with his own property, so he should not be forced to remove. The Hanafi response (from the apparent narration) analogizes the buyer to a mortgagor who mortgages his property: if the mortgagor builds on the mortgaged property, his building can be overridden because the mortgagee has a superior right. Similarly, the pre-emptor's right is a strong right that attaches from the moment of sale, so the buyer's improvements are subject to that right and can be removed. In contrast, a donee (recipient of gift) or a buyer in a fasid purchase has permission from the owner, so their improvements are not revocable. Thus, the buyer here is like a usurper (ghāsb) because his right is conditional.

Key Terms
البناء والغرس (al-binā' wa al-ghars): Buildings and plantings made by the buyer on the land subject to pre-emption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if the buyer built a house on the land before the pre-emptor takes it, what can the pre-emptor do?
A) He must pay the buyer for the entire value of the building.
B) He can force the buyer to remove the building and take the land empty.
C) He must abandon his right to pre-emption if the buyer refuses to remove.
D) He can only take the land after paying the price and the building's value.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view gives the pre-emptor the powerful option to force removal of any improvements built without permission, reflecting the strength of the shuf'a right.
