Pre-emptor's Right to Recoup Improvements After Land is Claimed
Ruling on pre-emptor's recourse if he built then land was reclaimed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pre-emptor's right to recover the value of improvements when the pre-empted property is reclaimed, and explain the legal basis distinguishing the pre-emptor from a deceived buyer.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor who improves land before a claim can recover the improvement's value because he took by compulsion, not deception.

This lesson examines a crucial rule protecting the pre-emptor from loss when, after establishing a building or plantation on the pre-empted land, a third party proves superior title and demands removal. While the pre-emptor must remove, he does not bear the cost—he can reclaim the value from the seller or buyer. The key distinction hinges on the pre-emptor's involuntary acquisition versus a deceived buyer's voluntary transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the pre-emptor's actions
The pre-emptor takes land via shuf'a, builds or plants. Later, a rightful claimant (mustahaqq) emerges and forces removal.
The improvements are made in good faith before any claim.
2
State the pre-emptor's right to recoup improvement value
The pre-emptor can recover the value of the building/planting from the person from whom he took the land (buyer or seller).
This includes both the purchase price and the value of improvements.
3
Explain the legal basis: no gharar or tasleeth
The pre-emptor is not a maghrur (deceived buyer); he acquired compulsorily, so he is not bound by the defect. The person from whom he took the land is liable.
The buyer was deceived by the seller, but the pre-emptor was not deceived.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's agreement and the authority
Abu Yusuf supports this ruling, and it is cited from Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
This strengthens the reliability of the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فعلهما الشفيع شرط على الأرض المشفوعة فيها فيأخذها: أي الدار بعد مضي الأجل. وبمثل الخمر إلخ : أي أخذها الشفيع بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير إن كان الشفيع ذميا، صورته: اشترى ذمي من ذمي عقارا بخمر أو خنزير فإن كان شفيعه ذميا، أخذه بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير؛ لأن هذا فيما بينهم، فإذا صح ترتب عليه أحكام البيع مثل الشفعة ونحوها غير أن الذمي لا يتعذر صحيح عليه تسليم الخمر، فيأخذه بها؛ لأنها من ذوات الأمثال والخنزير من ذوات القيم، فيجب عليه قيمته. [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٠/٢] ولو كان الشفيع مرتدا لا شفعة له، سواء كان قتل على ردته أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب، ولا لورثته؛ لأن الشفعة لا تورث كذا في "العناية". (فتح) وبقيمتهما لو مسلما أي أخذ الدار بقيمة الخمر وقيمة الخنزير لو كان الشفيع مسلما؛ لأنه لا يقدر على تسليم المثل؛ لكونه ممنوعا عن تمليكهما، فيجب عليه قيمتهما كما في ضمان العدوان، وإن كان الشفيع مسلما وذميا، أخذ كل واحد منهما النصف بما ذكرنا من قيمة الخمر أو مثلها، ولو أسلم الذمي صار حكمه حكم المسلم من الابتداء، فيأخذها بالقيمة، والمستأمن كالذمي في جميع ذلك. عيني بتصرف من المحشي) وإن فعلهما إلخ يعني أن الشفيع إذا أخذ الأرض بالشفعة، فبنى أو غرس ثم استحقت، فكلف المستحق الشفيع بالقلع، فقلع البناء والغرس، رجع الشفيع على المشتري إن أخذها منه، أو على البائع إن أخذها منه بالثمن، بقيمة البناء والغرس وعن أبي يوسف عليه: أنه يرجع بذلك، والفرق بينه وبين المشتري مغرور من جهة البائع، ومسلط عليه من جهته، ولا غرور ولا تسليط للشفيع من جهة المشتري ولا البائع؛ لأن الشفيع أخذها منه جبرا تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٩/٨] ولا يرجع أن المشتري
And if the pre-emptor does them (builds or plants) after taking the land subject to pre-emption, then he takes it: i.e., the house after the term expires. ... (repeated material) ... 'And if the pre-emptor does them' etc.: meaning that if the pre-emptor took the land by shuf'a, then built or planted, then the land was claimed (by someone with a superior right), and the claimant forced the pre-emptor to remove, and he removed the building and planting, the pre-emptor has recourse against the buyer if he took it from him, or against the seller if he took it from him, for the price (he paid) and the value of the building and planting. According to Abu Yusuf, may (Allah have) mercy on him: he does have recourse for that. The difference between him and a buyer (who is deceived by the seller) is that the buyer is deceived by the seller and is given possession by him, but there is no deception or giving possession by the seller or buyer to the pre-emptor; because the pre-emptor took it from him by compulsion. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/269) And he does not have recourse (?) ... the buyer.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding the pre-emptor's right to compensation if, after exercising his shuf'a and making improvements (building or planting), the property is later reclaimed by a rightful owner (e.g., due to a title defect). The pre-emptor must remove the improvements at the claimant's demand, but he can then recover the value of those improvements from the person from whom he took the property (either the buyer or the seller). Abu Yusuf agrees with this ruling. The reasoning is that the pre-emptor is not like a deceived buyer (maghrūr); the buyer was misled by the seller, but the pre-emptor acquired the property compulsorily, so he should not bear the loss. The pre-emptor can claim back not only the price he paid but also the value of the improvements made in good faith. The text ends abruptly with 'ولا يرجع أن المشتري' which may mean that the pre-emptor does not have recourse against the buyer in a particular scenario, but the sentence is incomplete.

Key Terms
مغرور (maghrūr): One who is deceived in a transaction; here, a buyer who relies on the seller's assurance of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pre-emptor takes the land by shuf'a, builds a house, and then a third party proves rightful ownership and forces removal, from whom can the pre-emptor recover the value of the house?
A) Only from the buyer, not the seller.
B) Only from the seller, not the buyer.
C) From either the buyer or the seller, depending on whom he took the land from.
D) From the claimant who forced removal.

Key Takeaway
A pre-emptor who builds or plants on pre-empted land before a claim can recover the value of those improvements from the seller or buyer, because he took the land by compulsion and is not a deceived buyer.

Pre-emption with Unlawful Consideration: Wine and Pig
Ruling on shuf'ah with wine or pig as consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which shuf'ah applies when the sale consideration is wine or pig, and apply the ruling for dhimmi and Muslim pre-emptors.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah attaches even to sales of wine or pig among dhimmis, but Muslims must pay the value.

This passage examines a nuanced scenario: a dhimmi buys real estate with wine or a pig, and another party seeks pre-emption. The ruling hinges on the validity of the underlying sale among dhimmis and the status of the pre-emptor. Understanding this helps clarify how Hanafi fiqh handles property rights when the consideration is religiously prohibited, balancing validity of contracts among non-Muslims with Islamic prohibitions for Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its parties
The act is the exercise of shuf'ah (pre-emption) after a sale of real estate between two dhimmis, where the price is wine or a pig. Determine who the pre-emptor is: dhimmi, Muslim, apostate, or musta'min.
The sale itself is valid among dhimmis; this is the foundation.
2
Classify the consideration
Wine is a fungible (mithli) among dhimmis, pig is a valuable (qimi). Note that for Muslims, both are prohibited from ownership, so they cannot take the like.
Mithli vs qimi affects how compensation is paid.
3
Apply the rule based on pre-emptor's status
If the pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he takes with the same wine (like) and value of the pig. If Muslim, he takes with the value of both. If one is Muslim and one dhimmi (joint ownership), each takes half accordingly. Apostate has no right.
The ruling follows from the prohibition of Muslims owning wine/pig.
4
Consider edge cases
If the dhimmi pre-emptor converts to Islam, his taking switches to value. A musta'min is treated like a dhimmi. Pre-emption is not inherited (e.g., apostate's heir).
Conversion changes the ruling retroactively from the point of pre-emption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيأخذها: أي الدار بعد مضي الأجل. وبمثل الخمر إلخ : أي أخذها الشفيع بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير إن كان الشفيع ذميا، صورته: اشترى ذمي من ذمي عقارا بخمر أو خنزير فإن كان شفيعه ذميا، أخذه بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير؛ لأن هذا . فيما بينهم، فإذا صح ترتب عليه أحكام البيع مثل الشفعة ونحوها غير أن الذمي لا يتعذر صحيح عليه تسليم الخمر، فيأخذه بها؛ لأنها من ذوات الأمثال والخنزير من ذوات القيم، فيجب عليه قيمته. [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٠/٢] ولو كان الشفيع مرتدا لا شفعة له، سواء كان قتل على ردته أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب، ولا لورثته؛ لأن الشفعة لا تورث كذا في "العناية". (فتح) وبقيمتهما لو مسلما أي أخذ الدار بقيمة الخمر وقيمة الخنزير لو كان الشفيع مسلما؛ لأنه لا يقدر على تسليم المثل؛ لكونه ممنوعا عن تمليكهما، فيجب عليه قيمتهما كما في ضمان العدوان، وإن كان الشفيع مسلما وذميا، أخذ كل واحد منهما النصف بما ذكرنا من قيمة الخمر أو مثلها، ولو أسلم الذمي صار حكمه حكم المسلم من الابتداء، فيأخذها بالقيمة، والمستأمن كالذمي في جميع ذلك.
He takes it: meaning the house after the term expires. And with the like of wine etc.: meaning the pre-emptor takes it with the like of wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is a dhimmi. Its scenario: a dhimmi bought real estate from a dhimmi for wine or a pig; if his pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he takes it with the like of wine and the value of the pig, because this... among them. So if the sale is valid, its rulings follow, like pre-emption and similar, except that the dhimmi is not prevented from delivering wine, so he takes it with it because wine is of the category of fungibles, and the pig is of the category of valuables, so he must pay its value. And if the pre-emptor is an apostate, he has no pre-emption, whether he was killed for his apostasy, or died, or joined the land of war; nor do his heirs have it, because pre-emption is not inherited, as in al-'Ināyah. And with their value if Muslim: meaning he takes the house with the value of the wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is a Muslim, because he cannot deliver the like, being prohibited from owning them, so he must pay their value as in tort liability. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim and a dhimmi (jointly), each takes half as we mentioned, of the value of wine or its like. If the dhimmi converts to Islam, his ruling becomes like that of a Muslim from the beginning, so he takes it by value. The musta'min is like a dhimmi in all this.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on pre-emption (shuf'ah) when the consideration for the sale is wine or a pig. The author explains that if a dhimmi buys property from another dhimmi using wine or a pig as payment, the sale is valid among them. Consequently, pre-emption applies. If the pre-emptor is also a dhimmi, he may take the property by paying the same wine (since wine is a fungible) and the value of the pig. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim, he cannot take wine or pig, so he must pay their market value. The same applies to a musta'min (safe-conduct holder). An apostate has no right of pre-emption, and this right is not inherited. The reasoning is that among dhimmis, transactions with wine and pig are valid; thus shuf'ah follows. For Muslims, the prohibition on owning these items necessitates valuation. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, and the mufta bihi position is as stated.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to an outsider, under certain conditions.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who enjoys protection in exchange for jizyah.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim granted temporary safe conduct (aman) to enter Muslim lands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He takes it with the same wine, because the sale is valid.
B) He takes it with the value of the wine, because he cannot own wine.
C) He cannot take it because the sale is invalid.
D) He takes it with the value of the land as estimated by experts.

Key Takeaway
Among dhimmis, sales with wine or pig are valid, so shuf'ah attaches; Muslims must pay value; apostates lose the right.

Pre-emptor's Option When Buyer Has Built or Planted
Ruling on pre-emptor's choice when buyer has built or planted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pre-emptor's options under Hanafi law and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding forced removal of the buyer's improvements.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor can force a buyer to remove buildings or trees, because the pre-emptor's right is prior and strong.

This passage addresses a common scenario: the buyer of land subject to pre-emption (shuf'ah) constructs a building or plants trees before the pre-emptor claims his right. What happens when the pre-emptor later wins the case? The Hanafi school provides specific options, and there is a well-known disagreement between Abu Hanifa (and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this ruling clarifies the strength of the pre-emptor's right and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-emptor's two options according to Zahir al-Riwayah
Read the passage carefully. The pre-emptor may either take the land with the improvements by paying their value (as if uprooted), or force the buyer to remove them and take the land vacant.
The value of improvements is assessed 'as uprooted' (mashhadh al-qal'), meaning depreciated.
2
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf held that the buyer cannot be forced to remove improvements. The pre-emptor only has the option to take with compensation or leave the property altogether.
This view is also attributed to Shafi'i and Maliki.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil) for the Zahir al-Riwayah position
The text draws an analogy to a mortgagor building on mortgaged property: the mortgagee's right is prior, so the building is subject to removal. Similarly, the pre-emptor's right attaches to the land from the moment of sale, so any disposition by the buyer is vulnerable.
The pre-emptor's right is described as 'strong' (mut'akkad).
4
Examine the distinction from cases with permission
The passage contrasts the buyer with a donee (al-mawhub lahu) or a buyer in a void sale (shira' fasid): those act with the owner's permission, so their constructions are not overturned. The buyer in a valid sale does not have such permission from the pre-emptor.
This distinction highlights why the pre-emptor's right overrides.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The text explicitly states 'wa wajhu zahir al-riwayah' and the commentary confirms it is the relied-upon position. Thus, in fatwa, the pre-emptor can force removal.
This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالثمن إلخ : يعني إذا بنى المشتري في الأرض المشفوعة، ثم قضي للشفيع بالشفعة، فالشفيع بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعا، وإن شاء كلف المشتري قلعهما، فيأخذ الأرض فارغة، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يكلف بالقلع، ولكنه بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس، وإن شاء ترك، وبه قال الشافعي ومالك؛ لأنه ليس متعديا في البناء والغرس؛ لثبوت ملكه فيه بالشراء، فلا يعامل بأحكام العدوان، فصار كالموهوب له والمشتري شراء فاسدا عند الإمام، ووجه ظاهر الرواية : أنه بنى في محل تعلق به حق متأكد لغيره من غير تسليط منه فينتقض كالراهن إذا بنى في المرهون ولهذا تنتقض جميع تصرفات المشتري حتى الوقف والمسجد والمقبرة بخلاف الموهوب على قول أبي حنيفة والمشتري شراء فاسدا؛ لأنه فعل بتسليط المالك، من ولهذا لا ينتقض تصرفهما تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٦٨/٨]
And with the price etc.: meaning if the buyer built in the land subject to pre-emption, then the pre-emptor is awarded the pre-emption, the pre-emptor has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it with the price and the value of the building and plantation as uprooted; if he wishes, he forces the buyer to remove them, thus taking the land empty. From Abu Yusuf: he does not force removal, but he has the choice whether to take with price and value of building and plantation, or leave it. Imam al-Shafi'i and Malik also said this, because the buyer is not a wrongdoer in building/planting, having ownership by purchase, so he is not treated as a tortfeasor. Thus he is like the donee and the buyer in a void sale according to Abu Hanifa. The Zahir al-Riwayah position is that he built in a place where a strong right of another is attached, without his permission, so it is subject to removal, like the mortgagor who builds on the mortgaged property. For this reason all dispositions of the buyer are overturned, even waqf, mosque, and graveyard, unlike the donee according to Abu Hanifa and the buyer in a void sale, because they act with the owner's permission, so their dispositions are not overturned. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/368)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the buyer of land subject to pre-emption has built a structure or planted trees before the pre-emptor's claim. The pre-emptor, upon winning the case, has two choices: either take the land along with the building/planting by paying their value (as if they were uprooted/demolished), or force the buyer to remove them and take the land vacant. This is the position of the school (Zahir al-Riwayah). Abu Yusuf, however, held that the buyer cannot be forced to remove; instead the pre-emptor may either take with compensation or leave the property. The reasoning for the school's position is that the buyer built on land that is already encumbered by another's strong right (the pre-emptor's right), and without authorization, so it is like the mortgagor building on mortgaged property; the structures are subject to removal. The Shaybani further distinguishes from cases where the builder had permission (like donee or buyer in a void sale), where the structures remain. The mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Key Terms
البناء والغرس (al-bina' wa al-ghars): Building and planting (trees) on the land subject to pre-emption.
قلع (qal'): Removal (of building or trees) from the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must take the land with the building and pay its full market value.
B) He may either take the land with compensation for the building (as uprooted) or force the buyer to remove it.
C) He must leave the property to the buyer.
D) He can only take the land without paying anything for the building.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah) gives the pre-emptor the powerful option to force removal of the buyer's improvements, reflecting the strength of the pre-emptor's prior right.

Pre-emptor's Recourse After Building on Reclaimed Property
Ruling on pre-emptor's recourse after building on reclaimed property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pre-emptor's recourse when property is reclaimed after building/planting and identify the basis of compensation (ghurur).

Core Idea
A pre-emptor who builds on reclaimed property can recover the value from the party who sold him the land.

This lesson examines a scenario where a pre-emptor, after taking land by shuf'ah, makes improvements (building or planting) only to have a third party prove ownership (istihqaq) and compel removal. The question arises: can the pre-emptor recover the value of his improvements? The Hanafi ruling provides a clear answer based on the principle of ghurur (deception).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and actions
The pre-emptor takes land by shuf'ah, then builds or plants. Later, a rightful owner (mustahiqq) appears and forces removal.
2
Determine the legal consequence
The pre-emptor must remove the building/plantation. The key question is whether he can recover compensation.
3
Identify the right to recover
The pre-emptor can recover the value of the building/plantation from the party from whom he took the land (buyer or seller).
This is the Hanafi position, agreed by Abu Yusuf.
4
Analyze the reasoning (ghurur vs. compulsion)
The buyer is deceived (maghrur) by the seller, but the pre-emptor is not deceived by either party because he took the land by compulsion (jabr). Hence he recovers from the party he dealt with.
The distinction rests on the absence of deception from the buyer/seller toward the pre-emptor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فعلهما إلخ يعني أن الشفيع إذا أخذ الأرض بالشفعة، فبنى أو غرس ثم استحقت، فكلف المستحق الشفيع بالقلع، فقلع البناء والغرس، رجع الشفيع على المشتري إن أخذها منه، أو على البائع إن أخذها منه بالثمن، بقيمة البناء والغرس وعن أبي يوسف عليه: أنه يرجع بذلك، والفرق بينه وبين المشتري مغرور من جهة البائع، ومسلط عليه من جهته، ولا غرور ولا تسليط للشفيع من جهة المشتري ولا البائع؛ لأن الشفيع أخذها منه جبرا تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٩/٨]
And if he does them etc.: meaning if the pre-emptor took the land by pre-emption, then built or planted, then the land was reclaimed (istihqaq), and the rightful claimant compelled the pre-emptor to remove [the building/planting], so he removed the building and plantation, then the pre-emptor recovers from the buyer (if he took from him) or from the seller (if he took from him) the value of the building and plantation. And from Abu Yusuf: that he recovers that. The difference between him and the buyer is that the buyer is deceived (maghrur) by the seller and authorized by him; but the pre-emptor has no deception or authorization from the buyer or seller, because he took the land by compulsion. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/269)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage deals with a different scenario: the pre-emptor, after taking the land by shuf'ah, builds or plants on it. Later, a third party proves rightful ownership (istihqaq) and forces the pre-emptor to remove the structures. The pre-emptor can then recover the value of the building/plantation from the buyer (if he took the land from the buyer) or from the original seller (if he took from the seller). This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf also agrees on the recovery. The reasoning is that the buyer who sold the land to the pre-emptor had deceived the pre-emptor (ghurur) by selling a property that was not his, but the pre-emptor's position is unlike the buyer's because the buyer acted voluntarily while the pre-emptor was compelled. Thus, the pre-emptor's recourse is against the person from whom he took the land, i.e., the buyer or seller. The difference between the buyer and the pre-emptor: the buyer is deceived by the seller and has authorization from him; the pre-emptor has no such relation—he took by force. This ruling is part of the Zahir al-Riwayah, and the mufta bihi position is as stated.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Reclamation: a third party proves rightful ownership over property, invalidating the previous sale.
غرور (ghurur): Deception: a seller sells property he does not own, leading to loss for the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pre-emptor builds on land taken by shuf'ah and then a third party proves ownership (istihqaq) and forces removal, from whom can the pre-emptor recover the value of the building?
A) From the third party who reclaimed the land.
B) From the buyer (if the pre-emptor took from him) or from the original seller.
C) From no one; he bears the loss himself.
D) From the state treasury (bayt al-mal).

Key Takeaway
A pre-emptor who improves reclaimed property is entitled to compensation from the party who sold it to him, based on the principle of ghurur.

Full Price for Pre-emption When Subordinate Items Destroyed
Ruling on price for pre-emption when subordinates are destroyed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a pre-emptor pays the full price when buildings or trees are destroyed naturally, and explain the legal reasoning based on the principle of tābi' (subordination).

Core Idea
Subordinate items (buildings/trees) do not reduce the pre-emption price.

This lesson examines a specific case in pre-emption (shuf'a): what happens when the building or trees on the land are destroyed by natural causes before the pre-emptor takes possession. The Hanafi school holds that the pre-emptor must still pay the full price. This ruling rests on the principle that buildings and trees are legally subordinate (tābi') to the land; they have no independent price share. The passage also contrasts this with loss of the land itself, which does reduce the price proportionally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The buyer purchased land containing a house or trees. Before the pre-emptor exercises the right, the house collapses or trees wither due to natural causes (e.g., fire, storm, drought).
Natural destruction only – not human action.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The pre-emptor takes the property by paying the full original price, with no reduction for the destroyed building or trees.
3
Understand the Legal Reasoning (ʿIllah)
Buildings and trees are legally subordinate (tābiʿ) to the land. They enter the sale implicitly without being mentioned and no part of the purchase price is allocated to them. Therefore, their destruction does not affect the price.
The principle: al-tābiʿ lā yuqābilu shayʾan min al-thaman.
4
Contrast with Destruction of the Land (Principal)
If part of the land itself perishes (e.g., by flood), the price is reduced proportionally because the land is the original subject (aṣl) and has its own price share.
This contrast reinforces the rule for subordinates.
5
Apply to Murabaha Sale
Because the building/trees have no price share, the seller can sell the land on a Murabaha basis (cost-plus) without disclosing the value of the destroyed items.
Practical implication for trade.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبكل الثمن إلخ: يعني لو اشترى أرضا فيها دار أو غرس فخربت الدار من غير صنع سواء كان بالانهدام أو الاحتراق أوجف الشجر بغير صنع أحد يأخذها الشفيع بكل الثمن، ولا يسقط من الثمن شيء؛ لأنهما تابعان للأرض حتى يدخلان في البيع من غير ذكر فلا يقابلهما شيء من الثمن، ولهذا يبيعها في هذه الصورة مرابحة بلا بیان، بخلاف ما إذا تلف بعض الأرض بغرق حيث يسقط من الثمن بحصته؛ لأن الفائت بعض الأصل، هذا إذا انهدم البناء، ولم يبق له نقض ولا من الشجر شيء من حطب أو خشب. وأما إذا بقي شيء من ذلك، فلا بد من سقوط بعض الثمن، فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الدار يوم العقد، وعلى قيمة الأخذ. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٩/٨] ولو لم يأخذ النقض المشتري كأن هلك بعد انفصاله، لم يسقط من الثمن شيء؛ لعدم حبسه؛ إذ هو من التوابع والتوابع لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن، وبالأخذ بالشفعة تحولت الصفقة إلى الشفيع، فقد هلك ما دخل تبعا قبل القبض، ولا يسقط بمثله شيء من الثمن. (من العيني والفتح)
And with the full price etc.: meaning if one bought land containing a house or plantation, and the house collapsed from no human action, whether by falling or fire, or the trees withered from no one's act, the pre-emptor takes it with the full price, and nothing of the price is reduced, because the house and trees are subordinate to the land, so they enter the sale without being mentioned, and nothing of the price corresponds to them. For this reason, he can sell it on Murabaha without stating the cost breakdown. Unlike if some of the land itself perished by flooding, where the price is reduced proportionally, because what was lost is part of the principal. This is if the building collapsed and no materials remain, and of the trees no firewood or timber remains. But if something of that remains, then a part of the price must be reduced, and the price is divided according to the value of the house at the time of the contract and according to the value of the taking. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/269) And if the buyer did not take the materials, as if they were lost after separation, nothing of the price is reduced because there was no retention (habs), as they are of the subordinates and subordinates are not met with any portion of the price. By taking by pre-emption, the transaction shifts to the pre-emptor; what entered as a subordinate perished before possession, and nothing of the price is reduced thereby. (From al-'Ayni and al-Fath)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where the building or trees on the purchased land are destroyed naturally (by collapse, fire, or withering) before the pre-emptor takes the property. The ruling is that the pre-emptor still pays the full purchase price, with no reduction. The reason is that buildings and trees are legally subordinate (tābi') to the land; they are not independent objects of sale and thus no part of the price corresponds to them. This is contrasted with destruction of part of the land itself (e.g., by flooding), where a proportional reduction applies. However, if any valuable remnants remain (like bricks or timber), the price must be divided accordingly. Additionally, if the buyer already separated the materials but they were then lost (without his taking possession), no reduction occurs because the materials, being subordinate, have no allocated price. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
تابع (tābi'): Subordinate: an item that is not the primary subject of sale but is included by implication, like building on land.
مرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit margin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that determines whether the pre-emption price is reduced?
A) Whether the destruction was natural or human-caused
B) Whether the destroyed item is principal or subordinate
C) Whether the property is land or a building
D) Whether the pre-emptor is ready to pay

Key Takeaway
A pre-emptor pays the full price even when buildings or trees on the land are destroyed naturally, because they are subordinates with no share in the price.

Effect of Remnants and Lost Materials on Pre-emption Price
Exceptions to the full-price rule: remnants and lost materials

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on price reduction when remnants of a destroyed building or trees exist, and determine when no reduction applies despite loss of materials.

Core Idea
If remnants remain, price is reduced; if buyer lost materials after separation, no reduction.

This lesson covers two special scenarios within the pre-emption (shuf'a) rules. First, what happens if some parts of the destroyed building (bricks, timber) or tree remnants (firewood) survive? Second, what if the buyer separated the materials but then lost them before the pre-emptor took possession? The Hanafi jurists provide detailed rulings that maintain consistency with the principle that subordinates (tawābiʿ) do not have a price share, but remnants are considered independent property that can be valued.

Guided Path
1
Case 1: Remnants Exist
If after the destruction, some materials remain (e.g., bricks from a collapsed building, firewood from withered trees), the full price is not paid. Instead, the price is divided proportionally between the value of the land at the time of contract and the value of the remnants at the time of taking.
The remnants are no longer purely subordinate; they have independent value.
2
Case 2: Buyer Lost Materials After Separation
If the buyer separated the materials (e.g., took the bricks and stored them) but they were then lost (e.g., stolen or destroyed) without his taking possession, the pre-emptor pays the full price. No reduction occurs because the materials were never in the buyer's possession (no ḥabs), and they were still subordinates with no allocated price.
The key is whether the buyer had control (ḥabs) over the separated materials.
3
Understand the Legal Basis
Remnants: They are like a separate asset that the buyer still owns; the pre-emptor must pay for them. Lost materials: They were subordinates that perished before possession shifted to the pre-emptor, so no price reduction.
4
Apply the Principles
If remnants exist, value them at the time of taking and reduce the price accordingly. If materials were lost after separation without buyer's possession, no reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبكل الثمن إلخ: يعني لو اشترى أرضا فيها دار أو غرس فخربت الدار من غير صنع سواء كان بالانهدام أو الاحتراق أوجف الشجر بغير صنع أحد يأخذها الشفيع بكل الثمن، ولا يسقط من الثمن شيء؛ لأنهما تابعان للأرض حتى يدخلان في البيع من غير ذكر فلا يقابلهما شيء من الثمن، ولهذا يبيعها في هذه الصورة مرابحة بلا بیان، بخلاف ما إذا تلف بعض الأرض بغرق حيث يسقط من الثمن بحصته؛ لأن الفائت بعض الأصل، هذا إذا انهدم البناء، ولم يبق له نقض ولا من الشجر شيء من حطب أو خشب. وأما إذا بقي شيء من ذلك، فلا بد من سقوط بعض الثمن، فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الدار يوم العقد، وعلى قيمة الأخذ. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٩/٨] ولو لم يأخذ النقض المشتري كأن هلك بعد انفصاله، لم يسقط من الثمن شيء؛ لعدم حبسه؛ إذ هو من التوابع والتوابع لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن، وبالأخذ بالشفعة تحولت الصفقة إلى الشفيع، فقد هلك ما دخل تبعا قبل القبض، ولا يسقط بمثله شيء من الثمن. (من العيني والفتح)
And with the full price etc.: meaning if one bought land containing a house or plantation, and the house collapsed from no human action, whether by falling or fire, or the trees withered from no one's act, the pre-emptor takes it with the full price, and nothing of the price is reduced, because the house and trees are subordinate to the land, so they enter the sale without being mentioned, and nothing of the price corresponds to them. For this reason, he can sell it on Murabaha without stating the cost breakdown. Unlike if some of the land itself perished by flooding, where the price is reduced proportionally, because what was lost is part of the principal. This is if the building collapsed and no materials remain, and of the trees no firewood or timber remains. But if something of that remains, then a part of the price must be reduced, and the price is divided according to the value of the house at the time of the contract and according to the value of the taking. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/269) And if the buyer did not take the materials, as if they were lost after separation, nothing of the price is reduced because there was no retention (habs), as they are of the subordinates and subordinates are not met with any portion of the price. By taking by pre-emption, the transaction shifts to the pre-emptor; what entered as a subordinate perished before possession, and nothing of the price is reduced thereby. (From al-'Ayni and al-Fath)

This passage addresses the case where the building or trees on the purchased land are destroyed naturally (by collapse, fire, or withering) before the pre-emptor takes the property. The ruling is that the pre-emptor still pays the full purchase price, with no reduction. The reason is that buildings and trees are legally subordinate (tābi') to the land; they are not independent objects of sale and thus no part of the price corresponds to them. This is contrasted with destruction of part of the land itself (e.g., by flooding), where a proportional reduction applies. However, if any valuable remnants remain (like bricks or timber), the price must be divided accordingly. Additionally, if the buyer already separated the materials but they were then lost (without his taking possession), no reduction occurs because the materials, being subordinate, have no allocated price. Key Terms
نقض (naqḍ): Bricks or building materials that remain after a structure collapses.
حطب (ḥaṭab): Firewood from withered trees.
حبس (ḥabs): Retention or taking possession of an item.

Pre-emption When Buyer Demolishes the Building
Ruling on pre-emption price when buyer demolishes building

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of shuf'ah when the buyer demolishes the building and distinguish it from natural destruction.

Core Idea
When the buyer demolishes the building, the pre-emptor only takes the land at its share; the materials belong to the buyer.

In pre-emption, the right applies to the entire property. But what happens when the buyer, before the pre-emptor takes it, destroys the building? This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling: the demolition changes the nature of the transaction, and the pre-emptor's right is limited to the land. The reasoning is based on qiyas (analogy) with natural destruction and third-party actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each scenario
Read the source passage to find the ruling when the buyer demolishes, when the building collapses naturally, and when a third party demolishes.
2
Understand the reasoning (qiyas)
Notice that the passage uses analogy: demolition by buyer is like destruction by a third party, but different from natural destruction because the buyer's act is deliberate.
The key distinction is intentionality.
3
Compare with natural destruction
Contrast the ruling when the building collapses naturally (the pre-emptor takes everything, paying full price) with deliberate demolition (only land at share).
4
Apply to third-party demolition
Extend the ruling: if a stranger demolishes, the same rule applies as when the buyer demolishes.
Consistency in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Determine ownership of materials
Conclude that the demolished materials belong to the buyer, not the pre-emptor, because they became independent after separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبحصة العرصة إلخ يعني أخذ الشفيع العرصة بحصتها من الثمن إن نقض المشتري البناء؛ لأنه صار مقصودا بالإتلاف، ويقابله شيء من الثمن، فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الأرض والبناء يوم العقد عليهما، بخلاف الأول؛ لأن الهلاك فيه بآفة سماوية، وبخلاف ما إذا انهدم بنفسه، وكان النقض باقيا حيث يعتبر فيها قيمة النقض يوم بالشفعة؛ لأنه صار له قيمة بالحبس، ونقض الأجنبي البناء كنقض المشتري. وفي "التاتارخانية": لو لم يهدم المشتري البناء، ولكن باعه من غيره من غير إرضاء، ثم حضر الشفيع، فله أن ينقض البيع، ويأخذ الكل، وكذا النبات والنخل. (من العيني والتكملة) والنقض له [بكسر النون بمعنى المنقوض كالنكث بمعنى المنكوث ويضم أيضا وجاء النقض بفتحتين] يعني النقض للمشتري؛ لأن الشفيع إنما كان يأخذه بطريق التبعية للعرصة، وقد زالت بالانفصال تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۰/۸]
And by the share of the land etc.: meaning the pre-emptor takes the land by its share of the price if the buyer demolished the building, because it became an object of destruction, and a part of the price corresponds to it. So the price is divided according to the value of the land and the building on the day of the contract. Unlike the first case (natural destruction), because that destruction was by a heavenly affliction; and unlike if it collapsed by itself and the materials remained, where the value of the materials at the time of pre-emption is considered, because they acquired value by retention (habs). Demolition by a third party is like demolition by the buyer. In al-Tatarkhāniyyah: if the buyer did not demolish the building but sold it to another without satisfying [the pre-emptor], then the pre-emptor arrives; he may rescind that sale and take everything, and likewise for plants and palm trees. (From al-'Ayni and al-Takmilah) And the materials are his (the buyer's): meaning the demolished materials belong to the buyer, because the pre-emptor only takes them by way of subordination to the land, and that has ceased upon separation. (Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/270)
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the buyer actively demolishes the building (as opposed to natural destruction). Here, the pre-emptor takes only the land, paying a proportionate share of the purchase price. The price is divided based on the value of the land and building at the time of the contract. The reasoning is that the buyer's deliberate demolition makes the building an independent object that can be valued, unlike in natural destruction where it remains subordinate. The materials from demolition belong to the buyer, not the pre-emptor. Additionally, if the buyer sold the building to a third party, the pre-emptor can rescind that sale and take the entire property. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
العرصة (al-'arṣah): The land (ground) of the property, as distinct from buildings.
النقض (al-naqḍ): The materials of a demolished building; also the act of demolition.

Key Takeaway
When the buyer deliberately demolishes the building, the pre-emptor takes only the land at its share; the demolished materials belong to the buyer.

Pre-emptor Pays Full Price When Appurtenances Perish Naturally
Ruling on pre-emptor's right when property's appurtenances perish naturally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-emptor's payment when appurtenances (buildings/trees) are naturally destroyed, explain the underlying principle of taba', and distinguish it from destruction of the principal.

Core Idea
Appurtenances follow the land; their natural destruction does not reduce the price for the pre-emptor.

The pre-emptor has the right to take purchased property at the price paid by the buyer. But what happens if the building or trees on the land are destroyed before the pre-emptor takes possession? This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling that the pre-emptor pays the full price, because appurtenances are included in the sale without separate allocation. We will also explore the exceptions and underlying legal principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the pre-emptor taking property where appurtenances have been naturally destroyed. The ruling is that the pre-emptor pays the full price.
The act is valid; the price remains unchanged.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is istihsan, based on the principle that appurtenances (taba') are not priced separately.
This overrides any qiyas that would mandate reduction.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since appurtenances enter the sale without mention, no portion of the price corresponds to them; their destruction does not affect the price.
The legal link is the nature of taba' in Hanafi sales law.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools may reduce the price proportionally; Hanafi holds full price.
The ikhtilaf is on the legal characterization of appurtenances.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is the full price, as per Zahir al-Riwayah and the commentaries.
This is the mufta bihi for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها رجع بالثمن فقط، وبكل الثمن إن خربت الدار أو جف الشجر، جزاء أي أخذ الشفيع بكل الثمن وبكل الثمن إلخ: يعني لو اشترى أرضا فيها دار أو غرس فخربت الدار من غير صنع سواء كان بالانهدام أو الاحتراق أوجف الشجر بغير صنع أحد يأخذها الشفيع بكل الثمن، ولا يسقط من الثمن شيء؛ لأنهما تابعان للأرض حتى يدخلان في البيع من غير ذكر فلا يقابلهما شيء من الثمن، ولهذا يبيعها في هذه الصورة مرابحة بلا بیان، بخلاف ما إذا تلف بعض الأرض بغرق حيث يسقط من الثمن بحصته؛ لأن الفائت بعض الأصل، هذا إذا انهدم البناء، ولم يبق له نقض ولا من الشجر شيء من حطب أو خشب. وأما إذا بقي شيء من ذلك، فلا بد من سقوط بعض الثمن، فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الدار يوم العقد، وعلى قيمة الأخذ. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٦٩/٨] ولو لم يأخذ النقض المشتري كأن هلك بعد انفصاله، لم يسقط من الثمن شيء؛ لعدم حبسه؛ إذ هو من التوابع والتوابع لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن، وبالأخذ بالشفعة تحولت الصفقة إلى الشفيع، فقد هلك ما دخل تبعا قبل القبض، ولا يسقط بمثله شيء من الثمن. (من العيني والفتح)
The chapter of pre-emption: demand of pre-emption and litigation therein: he (the pre-emptor) gets back only the price, and the entire price if the house is ruined or the trees dry up. Explanation: i.e., the pre-emptor takes with the entire price. 'And with the entire price etc.': meaning if he bought land in which there is a house or plants, then the house is ruined without any act (of the buyer), whether by collapse or fire, or the trees dry up without anyone's act, the pre-emptor takes it with the entire price, and nothing of the price is dropped; because both (building and trees) are appurtenances to the land, such that they enter the sale without mention, so nothing of the price corresponds to them. For this reason, he sells it in this case as a profit sale without disclosure, contrary to the case when part of the land is destroyed by flood, where [the price] is dropped by its share; because the lost part is some of the original. This is when the building collapses and no debris of it remains, nor any wood or timber from the trees. But if something of that remains, then some of the price must drop, so the price is divided on the value of the house on the contract day and on the value of the taking. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/269] And if the buyer did not take the debris, as if it perished after separation, nothing of the price is dropped; because it is not detained (by the buyer) since it is from the appurtenances, and appurtenances have no portion of the price corresponding to them, and by taking via pre-emption the transaction is transferred to the pre-emptor, so what entered as an appurtenance perished before possession, and nothing of the price drops due to it. (From al-`Ayni and al-Fath).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq on the pre-emptor's right when the purchased property (land with house or trees) has been destroyed naturally before the pre-emptor takes possession. The ruling states that the pre-emptor pays the full price, because the building and trees are considered appurtenances to the land; they enter the sale without being specifically mentioned, so no part of the price is allocated to them. Therefore, their destruction does not reduce the price. The commentary elaborates: if the building collapses or trees dry up without human action, the pre-emptor takes the land at the full price. However, if part of the land itself is destroyed (e.g., by flood), the price is reduced proportionally because the land is the principal. Two sub-cases are discussed: (1) if debris or wood remains, the price must be divided between the land and the appurtenances based on their values at the time of the contract; (2) if the debris perishes after separation without the buyer taking possession, nothing is deducted because the debris was an appurtenance and never corresponded to any part of the price. The proof is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and the principle that appurtenances follow the principal in sale. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in the commentaries.

Key Terms
تبع (taba‘): An appurtenance that is included in a sale without being specifically mentioned, such as a building on land or fruit on a tree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The price of the land only, minus the value of the house
C) Only half the price
D) Nothing, the contract is void

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi pre-emption law, appurtenances (taba‘) are not allocated any portion of the price; their natural destruction does not reduce the pre-emptor's payment.

Pre-emptor's Right When Buyer Demolishes the Building
Ruling on pre-emptor taking land at share when buyer demolishes building

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the buyer demolishes the building before pre-emption and explain the rationale using qiyas khafi.

Core Idea
When the buyer demolishes the building, the pre-emptor takes only the land at its proportional share of the price.

Pre-emption (shuf'ah) gives a neighbor the right to buy property sold to another. But what happens if the buyer alters or demolishes the building before the pre-emptor claims? The Hanafi school provides a detailed ruling based on the principle that the buyer's intentional act changes the nature of the property. This lesson unpacks the law when the buyer demolishes the building, distinguishing it from natural destruction and clarifying ownership of debris.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the pre-emptor claiming the property after the buyer has demolished the building. The ruling from the matn: the pre-emptor takes only the land at its proportional share of the price.
This is the core hukm.
2
Understand the rationale (illah)
The buyer's demolition makes the building an 'object of destruction' (مقصود بالإتلاف). Therefore, part of the price corresponds to the building, so it must be separated. The price is divided proportionally based on values at contract date.
This distinguishes it from natural destruction.
3
Distinguish from natural destruction
If the building collapses by a heavenly calamity (آفة سماوية), the rule is different: the pre-emptor takes the property as it is because there is no intentional act. The illah (intent) is absent.
The text contrasts the two cases.
4
Consider ownership of debris (naqd)
The demolished material (naqd) belongs to the buyer, not the pre-emptor, because it has separated from the land and is no longer an appurtenance.
The term naqd is clarified linguistically.
5
Handle related scenarios
If the buyer sold the building separately to a third party, the pre-emptor can void that sale and take everything. If only part is demolished/cut, the pre-emptor takes the land and what remains.
These come from the subsidiary rulings in al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Khaniyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبحصة العرصة إن نقض المشتري البناء والنقض له، وبثمرها إن ابتاع أرضا جزاء أي وأخذها الشفيع إلخ وبحصة العرصة إلخ يعني أخذ الشفيع العرصة بحصتها من الثمن إن نقض المشتري البناء؛ لأنه صار مقصودا بالإتلاف، ويقابله شيء من الثمن، فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الأرض والبناء يوم العقد عليهما، بخلاف الأول؛ لأن الهلاك فيه بآفة سماوية، وبخلاف ما إذا انهدم بنفسه، وكان النقض باقيا حيث يعتبر فيها قيمة النقض يوم بالشفعة؛ لأنه صار له قيمة بالحبس، ونقض الأجنبي البناء كنقض المشتري. وفي "التاتارخانية": لو لم يهدم المشتري البناء، ولكن باعه من غيره من غير إرضاء، ثم حضر الشفيع، فله أن ينقض البيع، ويأخذ الكل، وكذا النبات والنخل. (من العيني والتكملة) والنقض له [بكسر النون بمعنى المنقوض كالنكث بمعنى المنكوث ويضم أيضا وجاء النقض بفتحتين] يعني النقض للمشتري؛ لأن الشفيع إنما كان يأخذه بطريق التبعية للعرصة، وقد زالت بالانفصال تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۰/۸] وفي "الخانية": لو اشترى قرية فيها أشجار ونخل فقطع المشتري بعض الأشجار، وهدم بعض البناء، فحضر الشفيع، يأخذ الأرض وما لم يقطع من الأشجار وما لم يهدم من البناء، وليس له أن يأخذها بالشفعة، ويقسم =
And with the share of the land if the buyer demolishes the building, and the debris is for him, and with its fruit if he bought land. Explanation: i.e., and he (the pre-emptor) takes it etc. 'And with the share of the land etc.' means the pre-emptor takes the land with its share of the price if the buyer demolishes the building; because he (the buyer) made it an object of destruction, and part of the price corresponds to it, so the price is divided on the value of the land and the building on the day of contract for both, contrary to the first case because the destruction there was by a heavenly calamity, and contrary to if it collapsed by itself and the debris remained, where the value of the debris at the time of pre-emption is considered; because it acquired value by detention (by the buyer). And demolition by a stranger is like demolition by the buyer. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the buyer did not demolish the building but sold it to someone else without consent, then the pre-emptor comes, he may void the sale and take everything, and likewise plants and date palms. (From al-`Ayni and al-Takmila) And the debris is for him (by kasr of the nun meaning demolished stuff like naqd meaning undone, and it can also be with damm, and it comes as naqd with two fatha) meaning the debris is for the buyer; because the pre-emptor would only take it by way of appurtenance to the land, and that [appurtenance] has ceased by separation. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/270] And in al-Khaniyya: If he bought a village in which there are trees and date palms, and the buyer cut some trees and demolished some building, then the pre-emptor comes, he takes the land and what was not cut of the trees and what was not demolished of the building, and he does not have the right to take it by pre-emption [not clear], and he divides =
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage addresses the pre-emptor's right when the buyer has positively altered the property by demolishing buildings or cutting trees. The ruling distinguishes this from natural destruction: here, the pre-emptor takes only the land at its share of the price, not the full price. The reason is that the buyer's act makes the building an 'object of destruction' so part of the price corresponds to it. The price is divided proportionally between land and building based on their values at the contract date. If an outsider demolishes, the same rule applies. The commentary from al-Tatarkhaniyya adds that if the buyer sold the building separately, the pre-emptor can void that sale and take everything. The debris (demolished material) belongs to the buyer because it has separated from the land and is no longer an appurtenance. The passage from al-Khaniyya gives a scenario of partial demolition and cutting: the pre-emptor takes the land and what remains of trees and buildings, dividing the price accordingly. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in later fatwa collections.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): Demolished material, debris; specifically the building materials after demolition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer demolishes the building on the land, how does the pre-emptor take the property?
A) Takes the land and building together at full price
B) Takes only the land at its share of the price
C) Cannot take the property at all
D) Takes the building at full price, land free

Key Takeaway
When the buyer demolishes the building, the pre-emptor takes only the land at its proportional share of the price, and the debris belongs to the buyer.

Pre-emptor's Right to Fruit and Trees
Ruling on pre-emptor taking fruit when land bought with fruit condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pre-emptor's right to fruit and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor takes fruit only if included by condition; istihsan treats connected growth as an appurtenance.

This lesson examines a nuanced rule of pre-emption (shuf'ah): when the buyer purchases land with its fruit, and the fruit appears after purchase, does the pre-emptor (shafi') take the fruit along with the land? The Hanafi school uses istihsan to grant this right, analogizing to an animal's offspring, overriding the qiyas that would exclude the fruit as a mere chattel.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the specific scenario: land bought with fruit by condition, fruit appears after purchase. The pre-emptor takes the fruit along with the land.
Ruling depends on the condition.
2
Analyze the qiyas
The qiyas would deny the fruit to the pre-emptor, comparing it to movable goods placed on the land. Recognize why qiyas fails here.
Qiyas would treat fruit as separate property.
3
Apply istihsan
Understand the istihsan: the fruit is connected by creation and generated from the sold property, analogous to a sold animal's offspring. The right to the original extends to what it generates.
Analogy to animal birth.
4
Verify the condition
Confirm that this only applies when fruit was explicitly stipulated in the sale. Without condition, the pre-emptor does not take the fruit.
Condition is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبثمرها إن ابتاع أرضا إلخ يعني يأخذها الشفيع مع ثمرها إن كان المشتري اشترى الأرض مع الثمر بأن شرطه في البيع، فأثمر عند المشتري بعد الشراء؛ لأن الثمر لا يدخل في البيع إلا بالشرط، بخلاف النخل، والقياس أن يكون له أخذ الثمر ؛ لعدم التبعية كالمتاع الموضوع فيها وجه الاستحسان أن الاتصال خلقة صار تبعا من وجه، وما يتولد من المبيع، فيسري إليه الحق الثابت في الأصل كالمبيع إذا ولدت قبل القبض، فإن المشتري يملك الولد تبعا للأم، وكذا هنا. الأخذ وفي "الخانية": لو اشترى قرية فيها أشجار ونخل فقطع المشتري بعض الأشجار، وهدم بعض البناء، فحضر الشفيع، يأخذ الأرض وما لم يقطع من الأشجار وما لم يهدم من البناء، وليس له أن يأخذها بالشفعة، ويقسم =
And with its fruit if he bought land etc. i.e., the pre-emptor takes it with its fruit if the buyer bought the land with the fruit by stipulating it in the sale, and it bore fruit with the buyer after the purchase; because fruit does not enter the sale except by condition, unlike date palms. The qiyas would be that he (the pre-emptor) should not have the right to take the fruit due to lack of appurtenance, like goods placed on it; the istihsan (preference) is that connection by creation makes it an appurtenance in some respect, and it is generated from the sold item, so the right established in the original extends to it, like if a sold animal gives birth before possession, the buyer owns the offspring as appurtenance to the mother. So here too. The taking. And in al-Khaniyya: If he bought a village in which there are trees and date palms, and the buyer cut some trees and demolished some building, then the pre-emptor comes, he takes the land and what was not cut of the trees and what was not demolished of the building, and he does not have the right to take it by pre-emption, and he divides =
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage deals with fruit and trees in the context of pre-emption. The ruling: if the buyer purchased land with fruit by stipulating it in the contract, and after the purchase the fruit appears, the pre-emptor takes the fruit along with the land. The reason is istihsan: even though fruit normally is not an appurtenance, because it is connected to the land by creation and is generated from the sold property, the pre-emptor's right extends to it. The qiyas would deny the pre-emptor the fruit, comparing it to movable goods, but istihsan overrides, analogizing to the case of a sold animal giving birth before delivery—the buyer owns the offspring as appurtenance. The text also includes a quote from al-Khaniyya about partial demolition and cutting, which is a repetition from lesson 2. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as interpreted in later works.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit that grows after purchase, included by condition in the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only if the fruit was stipulated in the sale contract
B) In all cases, because fruit is part of the land
C) Only if the fruit is still unripe
D) Never, fruit is separate property

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor takes fruit only when it was included by condition, based on istihsan analogizing to an animal's offspring.

Effect of Buyer's Acts on Pre-emption Right and Price Deduction
Ruling on price deduction for fruit and buildings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for deducting the value of fruit and building when a pre-emptor takes property, and distinguish between pre-existing fruit and fruit that appears after the sale.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor deducts the value of pre-existing fruit and buildings, but not fruit that appears after the sale.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines two scenarios affecting the pre-emptor's payment when the buyer has harvested fruit or built on the land. Distinguishing between what forms part of the original contract and what arises afterward is crucial for correct price calculation. We will explore the ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text discusses two cases: (1) fruit present at the time of sale and included by condition, then harvested or lost; (2) fruit that appears after the sale (new fruit). Determine the ruling for each.
2
Understand the condition for deduction
For the first scenario, the fruit is a principal (asl) because it was explicitly included. Therefore, if consumed, its value is deducted from the price. For the second, the fruit is a follower (tabi') and commands no separate price.
In Hanafi law, when an item is named in the sale, it becomes a principal.
3
Apply the building rule
If the buyer built on the land, the pre-emptor may demolish the building and deduct its value from the price. He pays only for the land (the 'plot' value).
The text says: 'what corresponds to the building falls, and what corresponds to the plot he takes it by.'
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially held that deduction should also apply to new fruit in the second scenario, but he later retracted. The final authoritative position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is no deduction for new fruit.
The text declares: 'and this statement is the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah).'
5
Recognize the authority weight
The ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school. Abu Yusuf's initial opinion is considered marjuh (weak) due to his retraction.
Zahir al-Riwayah represents the most reliable transmission from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جذه المشتري، سقطت حصته بأن شرط في البيع جزاء طلب الشفعة والخصومة فيها من أي حصة الثمر الثمن. - الثمن على قيمة البناء والأرض، فما أصاب البناء سقط، وما أصاب العرصة يأخذها به، وينقض بناء المشتري الذي أحدثه، وهذا القول ظاهر الرواية تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۰/۸] وإن جذه المشتري إلخ : أي إن قطع المشتري الثمر فيما إذا اشتراها بالثمن بالشرط، سقطت حصته أي حصة الثمر من الثمن؛ لأنه لما دخل بالتسمية صار أصلا، وكذلك إذا هلك بآفة سماوية، وأما في الفصل الثاني: وهو إذا أثمر عند المشتري ويأخذ الأرض والنخل بجميع الثمن؛ لأن الثمر لم يكن موجودا عند العقد، فلا يدخل عند الأخذ في البيع إلا تبعا، فلا يقابله شيء من الثمن، وكان أبو يوسف يقول أولا: يحط عنه من الثمن في الفصل الثاني أيضا، ثم رجع إلى ما ذكر في الكتاب [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤١/٢]
And if the buyer cuts it (the fruit), his share falls by (the condition in the sale of a portion of the fruit price...) The price is based on the value of the building and the land: what corresponds to the building falls, and what corresponds to the plot he takes it by, and he demolishes the buyer's building which he constructed, and this statement is the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 'And if the buyer cuts it etc.': i.e., if the buyer cuts the fruit when he purchased with the price condition, his share falls, i.e., the share of the fruit from the price, because when it entered by name it became a principal. And similarly if it perishes by a heavenly calamity. As for the second chapter: i.e., when fruit appears with the buyer, he takes the land and palm trees with the full price, because the fruit was not existent at the time of contract, so it does not enter into the sale upon taking except as a follower, so nothing of the price corresponds to it. Abu Yusuf used to say initially: deduction from the price in the second chapter as well, then he retracted to what is mentioned in the book [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 341/2].
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios affecting the pre-emptor's right when he takes the property. First, if the buyer purchased the property with the condition that the fruit (if any) is included, and then the buyer harvests that fruit before the pre-emptor takes it, the pre-emptor deducts the value of that fruit from the price he pays. This is because the fruit, by being explicitly named in the sale, became a principal part of the sale. Similarly, if the fruit perishes by an act of God, the pre-emptor deducts its value. Second, if the fruit appears on the trees after the buyer took possession but was not yet existent at the time of contract, the pre-emptor takes the land and trees at the full price without deducting for the fruit, because the fruit only follows the land and is not a principal part of the sale. The price is apportioned based on the values of the building and land: the part of the price corresponding to the building is dropped (since the pre-emptor can demolish it if he wishes), and the pre-emptor pays the part corresponding to the vacant land. Abu Yusuf initially held that a deduction should be made in the second scenario too, but he later retracted and agreed with the ruling of the book (which is that no deduction is made). The text explicitly states that this final position is the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit on trees, which may or may not be included in the sale depending on condition.
بناء (bina'): Building constructed by the buyer on the land.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted ruling from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The pre-emptor pays the full price including the fruit's value.
B) The pre-emptor deducts the value of the fruit from the price.
C) The pre-emptor does not have to take the fruit.
D) The pre-emptor takes the fruit for free.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor deducts the value of pre-existing fruit and buildings, but not fruit that appears after the sale.

Pre-emption (Shuf'ah) Applies Only to Immovable Property Acquired for Monetary Consideration
Definition of conditions for pre-emption obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for pre-emption to be obligatory in the Hanafi school and identify which properties are excluded.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is wajib only for immovable property bought with wealth—not for gifts, movable goods, or ships.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the scope of pre-emption (shuf'ah), a right of a co-owner or neighbour to purchase a sold share. The author clarifies that this right is not universal; it attaches only to immovable property ('aqar) that was acquired through a monetary consideration. We will unpack these conditions, examine the rationale behind them, and see how the Hanafi position differs from the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its level
The text states 'Pre-emption is obligatory (tajib al-shuf'ah)'. Determine the hukm level: it is wajib (obligatory) for the eligible party, but only when the conditions are met. Note the nuance: it is not a fard but a wajib right that can be forfeited.
Hanafi usul distinguishes fard (definitive obligation) from wajib (obligatory but with zanni proof).
2
List the conditions for obligation
Extract the two explicit conditions: (1) the property must be immovable ('aqar), and (2) it must have been acquired by a compensation that is wealth ('iwad huwa mal). Understand what each condition excludes.
The text uses particle 'illa' for restriction: 'illa fi 'aqar'.
3
Identify the exceptions and extensions
Note the exceptions: no pre-emption for movable goods ('ard), ships (fulk), or property acquired without monetary consideration (gift, dower, khul', etc.). Also note the secondary extension to upper storeys and trees due to permanent attachment.
The distinction 'qasdan' (primarily) vs 'taba'an' (secondarily) is key.
4
Analyse the madhhab disputes
The text contrasts Hanafi with Shafi'i (on divisible property) and Maliki (on ships). For each, identify the basis of difference: Shafi'i's concern is cost of division, Hanafi's is neighbourly harm; Maliki analogises ships to immovable property, Hanafi rejects because ships are movable.
The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'La shuf'ata illa fi rub' aw ha'it' interpreted as relative restriction.
5
Apply the definitions to concrete cases
Work through examples: a house bought with cash → pre-emption applies; a house received as a gift → no pre-emption; a ship purchased with money → no pre-emption; a well or pathway → pre-emption applies (since divisible? Hanafi says yes).
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) between schools is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يجب فيه الشفعة وما لا يجب إنما تجب الشفعة في عقار ملك بعوض هو مال، لا في عرض وفلك على صيغة المجهول لا تجب أي سفينة باب ما يجب فيه الشفعة إلخ لما ذكر بيان نفس وجوب الشفعة مجملا شرع في تفصيل ما تجب فيه الشفعة وما لا تحب؛ لأن التفصيل بعد الإجمال أوقع في النفس كذا في "العناية". [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۱/۸] إنما تجب الشفعة: [ أي لا تجب الشفعة قصدا إلا في العقار، وأما تبعا فيثبت في غيره أيضا كالشجر وغيره] أي تجب الشفعة في عقار سواء كان مما يقسم أو مما لا يقسم ولا تجب فيما سواه، وقال الشافعي: لا تجب فيما لا يقسم كالبئر والرحى والحمام والنهر والطريق وهذا الخلاف مبني على أصل عنده، وهو أن الشفعة تجب لدفع ضرر أجرة القسام عنده؛ إذ لا شفعة للجار عنده، بل للشريك، وعندنا لدفع ضرر الجوار على الدوام، ولا اختصاص لذلك بالمقسوم دون غيره. واحترز بقوله: "عوض" عما إذا ملكه بالهبة؛ فإن الشفعة لا تجب فيها، وبقوله: هو "مال" عما إذا ملك بعوض غير مال كالمهر، والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد والعتق وغيره؛ فإن الشفعة لا تجب في هذه الصور على ما بيناه قريبا. والعقار لغة الضيعة وقيل ما له أصل من دار أو ضيعة نقله الإمام المطرزي، ونقل الشراح هنا العقار: كل ما له أصل من دار أو ضيعة فهو مطابق للتفسير الثاني، والتفصيل في التكملة من التكملة والفتح وغيره) في عقار إلخ أي لا تثبت الشفعة قصدا إلا في العقار دون غيره، وإنما قلنا: "قصدا ؛ لأنها تثبت في غير العقار تبعا أيضا كالشجر والثمر وما في حكمه أي حكم العقار كالعلو؛ لأن حق التعلي يبقى على الدوام، فكان العلو كالعقار، قال في "الشرنبلالية": ثم إن كان العلو طريقه طريق السفل، يستحق الشفعة بالطريق على أنه خليط في الحقوق، وإن لم يكن بأن كان طريقه غير طريق السفل يستحقها بالمجاورة، وذكر "الديري" في "التكملة": أن القياس عدم وجوب الشفعة في العلو؛ لأنه لا يبقى على الدوام، وإنما استحسنوا؛ لأن حق الوضع متأبد فهو كالعرصة إلخ. (فتح) لا في عرض وفلك: يعني لا تجب الشفعة في عرض وفلك، وقال مالك: تجب في السفينة؛ لأنها تسكن كالعقار، ولنا: ما روي عنه عليه أنه قال: "لا شفعة إلا في ربع أو حائط"، ولأن الأخذ بالشفعة ثبت على خلاف القياس، فلا يجوز إلحاق المنقول به؛ لأنه ليس في معنى العقار تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۷۲/۸] ويرد على هذا الاستدلال أن ظاهر الحديث حصر ثبوت الشفعة في الربع والحائط فيدل على انتفاء الشفعة في عقار غير ربع وحائط كما دل على انتفائها في السفينة، ويمكن الجواب بحمل القصر على القصر الإضافي دون الحقيقي، فالقصر بالنسبة إليهما لا بالنسبة إلى جميع ما عداهما، فتأمل، قال في العناية الربع الدار والحائط البستان وأصله ما أحاط به. قوله: "عرض" بالسكون ما ليس بعقار فيكون ما بعده من عطف الخاص على العام (تكملة، فتح)
Chapter of what pre-emption is obligatory for and what not. Pre-emption is obligatory only for immovable property owned by a compensation that is wealth, not for goods and a ship (by the passive form: it is not obligatory, i.e., a ship). Chapter of what pre-emption is obligatory for etc. Since he mentioned the explanation of the obligation itself in summary, he began detailing what pre-emption is obligatory for and what is not, because detailing after summary is more impactful in the soul, as in al-'Inayah. [Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 271/8] 'Pre-emption is obligatory' means: It is not obligatory primarily except for immovable property, but secondarily it is established for other things like trees and others. It is obligatory for immovable property whether divisible or not, and not obligatory for anything else. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not obligatory for what is not divisible like well, mill, bath, river, and road. This difference is based on a principle with him, namely that pre-emption is obligatory to repel the harm of the divider's fee according to him, as there is no pre-emption for the neighbour according to him, but only for the partner. According to us (Hanafis), it is to repel the harm of permanent neighbourhood, and there is no specificity for divisible over others. He restricted with his saying 'compensation' to exclude if he owns it by gift, for pre-emption is not obligatory in it; and with his saying 'that is wealth' to exclude if he owns by a compensation that is not wealth, like dower, khul', settlement for intentional homicide, manumission, etc., for pre-emption is not obligatory in these cases as we explained recently. 'Al-'Aqar' linguistically means estate, and it is said: what has a foundation from a house or estate, transmitted by Imam al-Mutarrizi. The commentators here transmitted it as: every thing that has a foundation from a house or estate, which matches the second interpretation. Detail in al-Takmilah from al-Takmilah and al-Fath etc. 'In immovable property' etc. means: Pre-emption is not established primarily except in immovable property, but we said 'primarily' because it is established secondarily in other than immovable property like trees, fruit, and what is in their ruling, i.e., the ruling of immovable property like the upper storey, because the right of attachment remains permanently, so the upper storey is like immovable property. He said in al-Sharnablaliyyah: Then if the upper storey's access is via the lower storey, it deserves pre-emption by the access on the basis that he is a mixture in rights; if not, i.e., its access is different from the lower storey, then he deserves it by adjacency. Al-Dayri mentioned in al-Takmilah that analogy (qiyas) would require no pre-emption in the upper storey because it does not remain permanently, but they used istihsan because the right of placement is perpetual, so it is like the plot etc. (Fath) 'Not for goods and a ship' means: Pre-emption is not obligatory for goods and a ship. Malik said: It is obligatory for a ship because it settles like immovable property. Our proof: what is narrated from him (the Prophet) that he said: 'No pre-emption except in a quadratic (rub') or a walled garden (ha'it)'. And because taking by pre-emption is established contrary to analogy, so it is not permissible to annex movable property to it since it is not in the meaning of immovable property. [Completion of al-Bahr al-Raiq: 272/8] An objection is raised against this deduction: the apparent meaning of the hadith restricts the establishment of pre-emption to rub' and ha'it, indicating the negation of pre-emption in immovable property other than rub' and ha'it, just as it indicates its negation in a ship. It can be answered by interpreting the restriction as a relative restriction (qasr idafi) rather than absolute, so the restriction is with respect to them (i.e., rub' and ha'it) not with respect to everything else. Contemplate. He said in al-'Inayah: 'Rub'' means house, 'ha'it' means garden, its origin is what encloses it. His saying: 'ard (goods) by sukun means what is not immovable property, so what follows is a conjunction of specific to general. (Takmilah, Fath)
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This chapter defines the scope of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The core rule: Pre-emption is obligatory (wajib) only for immovable property ('aqar) that was acquired through a monetary consideration ('iwad mal). It does not apply to movable goods ('ard) or ships (fulk). The text explains that 'primarily' (qasdan) it applies only to immovable property, but secondarily (taba'an) it can apply to things attached to it like trees and fruit, and even upper storeys (al-'uluww) because they have a permanent right of placement. The Hanafi position is contrasted with al-Shafi'i, who restricts pre-emption to divisible property only, based on his view that pre-emption is to avoid the cost of division (ujrat al-qassam). The Hanafis hold that pre-emption is to avoid the harm of permanent neighbourhood (daf' darar al-jiwar 'ala al-dawam), which applies equally to divisible and indivisible property. Also contrasted with Malik, who extends pre-emption to ships, which the Hanafis reject because ships are movable and not in the meaning of 'aqar. The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'No shuf'ah except in rub' or ha'it.' The text addresses a potential objection: the hadith might seem to restrict pre-emption only to rub' (house) and ha'it (garden), excluding other immovable property like land. The answer is that the restriction is relative (qasr idafi) — it is meant to highlight these two common examples, not to exclude other immovable property. The text also excludes cases where property is acquired without monetary compensation, such as by gift, dower (mahr), khul', settlement for intentional homicide (sulh 'an dam 'amd), or manumission ('itq), because these are not considered compensatory wealth. Finally, it defines 'ard (goods) as anything not immovable.

Key Terms
عقار ('aqar): Immovable property, such as land, houses, and estates.
عوض ('iwad): Compensation or consideration in a contract, usually monetary.
عرض ('ard): Movable property, goods that are not immovable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, pre-emption (shuf'ah) is not applicable in which of the following?
A) A house purchased with money.
B) A piece of land received as a gift.
C) A date-palm grove purchased with money.
D) An upper storey attached to a house.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption (shuf'ah) is wajib only for immovable property ('aqar) that has been acquired through a monetary consideration ('iwad mal); it does not apply to movable goods, ships, or property acquired non-monetarily.
