Properties Subject to Shuf'a: Real Estate, Conditions, and Disputes
Ruling on types of property subject to pre-emption with conditions and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the types of property where shuf'a is wajib according to the Hanafi school, identify the conditions of compensation and property, and explain the Hanafi position on divisibility, movables, ships, and buildings with haqq al-qarar.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is wajib only in real estate owned for compensation, excluding movables, ships, and buildings on waqf land.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes the general rule for shuf'a and then unpacks its conditions, exceptions, and cross-madhhab differences. Understanding which properties trigger the right of pre-emption is crucial for both buyers and sellers in Islamic real estate transactions. The Hanafi school has a comprehensive approach that balances textual proofs, juristic preference (istihsan), and practical fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text opens with the rule: shuf'a is wajib only in real estate (aqar) that is owned by compensation (iwad) which is property (mal). Identify each element: 'aqar', 'iwad', 'mal'.
The term 'tajib' here means the right is established, not that it is obligatory to exercise.
2
Understand the conditions
Compensation excludes gifts; property excludes compensations like mahr, khul', sulh for intentional blood, etc. Note why these are excluded: they are not considered 'mal' in the sense of wealth.
This is a key distinction: shuf'a only arises when property is transferred for a countervalue that is measurable wealth.
3
Analyze the dispute on divisibility
The text contrasts the Hanafi position (shuf'a in all real estate, divisible or not) with al-Shafi'i's (only divisible). The difference stems from the underlying purpose: Hanafi sees it as repelling permanent neighbor harm, Shafi'i as preventing the cost of a divider.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf: under Shafi'i, wells, paths, etc. have no shuf'a; under Hanafi, they do.
4
Examine the extension to upper floors
Although upper floors (uluww) are not real estate by nature, Hanafis use istihsan to treat them as aqar because the right of attachment (haqq al-ta'liq) is permanent. Note the condition: the upper floor must have a stable right to exist.
This is a critical example of qiyas vs. istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Assess the rulings on buildings with haqq al-qarar and Meccan buildings
The text discusses buildings on waqf or sultani lands that have a 'right of stability'. Most later authorities (Bazzaziyya, Nihaya) rule no shuf'a because the right is not permanent. For Meccan buildings, the fatwa is shuf'a based on the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because the building itself is owned.
This shows how the fatwa may shift based on the nature of ownership and permanence.

Book of Pre-emption. What pre-emption is required for and what not. Chapter on what pre-emption is required for and what not. Verily pre-emption is required only in real estate owned by compensation that is wealth, not in movable goods and a ship. (Explanation: It is not required in movable goods and a ship). Chapter on what pre-emption is required for etc. The author clarified the details of what pre-emption is required for and what not. Pre-emption is required in real estate whether divisible or not, and not in other than it. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not required in what is not divisible like a well, mill, bathhouse, river, and road. This difference is based on a principle with him: that pre-emption is required to repel the harm of the divider's fee, since there is no pre-emption for a neighbor with him, but only for a partner. With us, it is to repel the harm of neighborliness permanently, and this is not specific to the divisible over other than it. The author restricts with 'compensation' for when one owns it by gift; then pre-emption is not required. And with his saying 'that is wealth' for when one owns by compensation that is not wealth, like mahr, khul', sulh for intentional blood, manumission, etc.; then pre-emption is not required. 'Real estate' linguistically is a landed property, or what has a foundation from a house or landed property. The commenters here say: real estate is everything that has a foundation from a house or landed property. Pre-emption is not established primarily except in real estate, but secondarily it is established in other than it as well, like trees and fruits and what is in its ruling, like the upper floor; because the right of attachment remains permanently, so the upper floor is like real estate. Then if the upper floor's pathway is the same as the lower floor's, it deserves pre-emption by way because of mixing in rights; if its pathway is different, it deserves it by adjacency. The measurement is that pre-emption is not required for the upper floor because it does not remain permanently, but they used juristic preference because the right of placement is perpetual. It is not required in movable goods and a ship. Malik said: It is required in a ship because it is stationary like real estate. Our proof: the hadith 'No pre-emption except in a house or garden'; and because taking by pre-emption is established against analogy, so it is not permissible to analogize movable property to it. There is a response to this deduction: the apparent meaning of the hadith restricts the establishment of pre-emption to a house and garden, so it indicates negation of pre-emption in real estate other than a house and garden, as it indicates negation in a ship. The answer is possible by interpreting the restriction as additive rather than true restriction. 'Movable' is what is not real estate, so what follows is specific from general. 'Building and palm trees sold without land' means pre-emption is not required in building and palm trees sold without land because they are movable; if sold with land, pre-emption is required in them secondarily. Unlike the upper floor where it deserves pre-emption and is used for pre-emption by adjacency. Know that the building that has a right of stability like building on leased waqf land and sultani land: is pre-emption required in it or not? There is lengthy discussion. Summary: Ibn al-Kamal held that building if sold with right of stability becomes like real estate, as did the upper floor, supported by Abu al-Su'ud. Others argued distinguishing: the building does not have permanent right of stability but is subject to removal. In al-Bazzaziyya and others: no pre-emption in it. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: sale of land of Mecca is not permissible, only sale of building, so no pre-emption; but Hasan ibn Ziyad reported from Abu Hanifa that it is required, and that is the position of the two companions and with it is fatwa. In Sharh al-Wahbaniyya: pre-emption there is established only on the basis that the land is owned, not merely because of building.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage from Kanzul Daqaiq with its commentary discusses the properties in which the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) is established. The author first states the general rule: shuf'a is wajib (established) only in real estate (aqar) that is owned by way of compensation ('iwad) which is property (mal). The compensation condition excludes gifts, and the property condition excludes non-wealthy compensations like mahr or khul'. The passage then addresses a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding indivisible property. Al-Shafi'i holds that shuf'a does not apply to indivisible properties like wells or paths, because his principle is that shuf'a only exists to prevent the harm of hiring a divider, and that only affects partners, not neighbors. The Hanafi position is that shuf'a exists to prevent the harm of neighborliness permanently, so it applies regardless of divisibility. The commentary then explains that shuf'a is primarily for real estate but secondarily applies to attached items like trees and upper floors. For upper floors, Hanafis use istihsan (juristic preference) to treat them as real estate because the right of placement is permanent. Next, the passage discusses movable goods ('arad) and ships (fulk). Against Imam Malik who includes ships, the Hanafi proof is the hadith 'No shuf'a except in a house or garden' (meaning real estate). The passage then deals with buildings and palm trees sold without land: they are movable and thus not subject to shuf'a unless sold with the land. A lengthy discussion follows about buildings on waqf or sultani lands that have a 'right of stability' (haqq al-qarar). Ibn al-Kamal and Abu al-Su'ud argued that such buildings, when sold with that right, are treated as real estate for shuf'a. However, the majority of later Hanafi authorities like al-Bazzaziyya, al-Nihaya, and al-Dhakhira rule against shuf'a in these cases, because the right of stability is not permanent—the tenant can be forced to remove the building if he fails to pay rent. The passage notes a fatwa from al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding Meccan land: since the land itself cannot be sold, only the building, shuf'a does not apply. But a riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa required shuf'a, and the fatwa is on the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) that it is required, because the building is owned. The commentator clarifies that this is based on the assumption that the land is owned, not just the building. The discussion illustrates the interplay of usul (textual proof), istihsan, and practical fatwa in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption; the legal right to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party, giving the privilege to the co-owner or neighbor to buy it instead.
عقار (aqar): Real estate such as land, buildings, houses, and gardens that are immovable by nature.
عرض (arad): Movable property or goods that are not real estate.
فلك (fulk): Ship or vessel; a movable property.
حق القرار (haqq al-qarar): Right of stability; the right to have a building stand on waqf or sultani land, akin to ownership of the structure but not the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which of the following properties is the right of shuf'a established?
A) A ship sold for money
B) A house sold for money
C) A building on waqf land sold with haqq al-qarar
D) A gift of a house

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes shuf'a only in real estate owned by compensation, with extensive rulings on attached properties and buildings on leased land, reflecting the principle of repelling permanent neighbor harm.

intermediate

Pre-emption in buildings and date palms sold without land
Explaining the ruling on pre-emption in buildings and date palms sold alone or with land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when pre-emption (shuf'ah) is established for a building or date palm sold with or without land, and distinguish the ruling for an upper floor sold alone.

Core Idea
A building or date palm sold alone is movable; only immovable property triggers pre-emption.

This lesson addresses a key distinction in Hanafi pre-emption law: when is a building or date palm considered movable or immovable for the purpose of shuf'ah? The answer depends on whether the land is included in the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Consider two sales: (1) building/date palm sold alone without land, (2) sold with land. Also note the special case of an upper floor (aluw) sold alone.
2
State the ruling for each
For scenario (1): no shuf'ah. For scenario (2): shuf'ah applies to the building/date palm as a subsidiary (taba'). For upper floor: shuf'ah applies even if sold alone.
3
Understand the reasoning (dalil)
The basis is the nature of the property: movable (manqul) vs immovable (aqar). A building/date palm sold alone is movable; sold with land it becomes part of the immovable. Upper floor is immovable due to permanent attachment.
The dalil is qiyas on the principle that shuf'ah attaches only to immovable property.
4
Distinguish the upper floor case
An upper floor (aluw) is considered immovable even when sold alone because it is permanently attached. Additionally, pre-emption may be claimed based on neighbor rights (mujawarah) or shared rights if the path is common.
This shows that attachment to land is not the only factor; immobility due to construction matters.
5
Summarize the conditions for shuf'ah
Shuf'ah requires the property to be immovable. If a building or tree is sold without land, it is treated as movable and no shuf'ah arises. If attached to land or permanently affixed (like an upper floor), shuf'ah applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبناء ونخل بيعا بلا عرصة . البناء والنخل: أي لا تحب الشفعة في بناء ونخل بيعا بلا عرصة؛ لأنهما منقولان فلا تجب فيهما إذا بيعا بلا أرض، وإن بيعا معها تجب فيهما الشفعة تبعا لها، بخلاف العلو حيث يستحق بالشفعة، وتستحق به الشفعة على أنه مجاورة، وذلك إذا كان طريقه غير طريق السفل، وإن كان طريقهما واحدا يستحق بالطريق الشفعة على أنه خليط في الحقوق.
And a building and date palms sold without ground. The building and date palms: meaning pre-emption is not established in a building and date palms sold without ground because they are movable, so it is not obligatory in them when sold without land. But if sold with it, pre-emption is obligatory in them as a follow-up to it. Unlike the upper floor, where pre-emption is claimed by it, and pre-emption is claimed on its basis as a neighbor, and that is when its path is different from the path of the lower floor. And if their path is one, he claims pre-emption by the path as a partner in rights.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with explanation from the commentator. The base text says 'a building and date palms sold without ground' meaning the ruling is that pre-emption (shuf'ah) does not apply to a building or date palm if sold alone without the land, because they are considered movable property (manqulat) in that case. Pre-emption is only established for immovable property (aqar). However, if the building or date palms are sold together with the land, pre-emption becomes established in them as a subsidiary (taba') to the land. The commentator then distinguishes this from the case of an upper floor (aluw): if an upper floor is sold alone, pre-emption is still established because it is considered a form of immovable property due to its attachment. And pre-emption can be claimed based on the upper floor itself, either on the ground of being a neighbor (mujawarah) if the upper floor has a separate entrance, or on the ground of sharing rights if they share the same entrance.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption right: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property sold to a third party by matching the price.
منقول (manqul): Movable property: something that can be transported without damage, like a building or date palm when sold without land.
العلو (al-'uluww): Upper floor or apartment: when sold separately, it still gives right to shuf'ah due to its immobile nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is pre-emption established in a building or date palm sold without land?
A) Always, because they are attached to land
B) Never, because they are movable
C) Only if sold with the land
D) Only if the buyer is a neighbor

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption in Hanafi law only applies to immovable property; a building or date palm sold alone is movable and not subject to shuf'ah unless sold with the land.

Pre-emption in buildings on waqf or sultani land with right of permanence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pre-emption in buildings on waqf or state land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on pre-emption for buildings with right of permanence on waqf or state land and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Pre-emption applies to immovables; buildings on non-owned land remain movable despite physical attachment.

This passage delves into a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school: does the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) apply to a building erected on waqf or state land when the builder holds a right of permanence (haqq al-qarar)? While some scholars analogize it to an upper floor (which triggers pre-emption), the relied-upon position denies pre-emption due to the building's legal status as movable property.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the relied-upon view from the text: there is no pre-emption in buildings on waqf or sultani land with right of permanence.
This is the mufta bihi according to the commentator and multiple authoritative sources.
2
Identify the opposing view
Ibn al-Kamal and Abu al-Su'ud argue that pre-emption does apply, analogizing to the upper floor.
They rely on the idea that the building with right of permanence is like real estate.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the dalil: analogy with the upper floor, and a narration by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifah about buildings in Makkah.
The narration is context-specific and later interpreted differently.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The commentator refutes by distinguishing the nature of the right: the building can be removed if rent is unpaid, unlike an upper floor. Also cites explicit texts from al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Nihayah, etc. denying pre-emption.
Key distinction: permanent vs terminable right of permanence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in fatwa, no pre-emption is granted for such buildings, affecting property transactions on leased lands.
This ruling is embedded in later Hanafi practice.

واعلم أن البناء والذي له حق القرار كالبناء في أرض الوقف المحتكرة والأرض السلطانية هل تجب فيه الشفعة أم لا؟ فيه كلام طويل ذكر في "الشامي" و"فتح المعين". حاصله: أن ابن الكمال جزم بأن البناء إذا بيع مع حق القرار كالبناء في الأرض السلطانية والوقف يلتحق بالعقار كما التحق العلو، وانتصره أبو السعود في حاشية الكنز وجزم بخطأ من أفتى بأنه لا شفعة في البناء في الأرض المحتكرة كالطوري؛ إذ لا سند له في فتواه، واستدل بما في شرح المجمع" لو بيع النخل وحده أو البناء وحده فلا شفعة؛ لأنهما إقرار لهما بدون العرصة، قال: فتعليله كالصريح في ثبوت الشفعة في البناء في المحتكرة لما له من حق القرار. واستدل أيضا بما روى الحسن بن زياد عن الإمام في بيع البناء بمكة كما سيأتي بيانه ورد على ابن الكمال أولا ببيان الفرق بين البناء المذكور وبين العلو وهو أن البناء فيما ذكر ليس له حق البقاء على الدوام، بل هو على شرف الزوال لما قالوا إن الأرض المحتكرة إذا امتنع المحتكر من دفع أجرة المثل يؤمر برفع بنائه وتؤجر لغيره، وكذا في الأراضي السلطانية إذا امتنع من دفع ما عينه السلطان، بخلاف حق التعلي؛ فإنه يبقى على الدوام، وثانيا بما في "البزازية" وغيره من عدم الشفعة فيه. قال في "البزازية" : ولا شفعة في الكردار أي البناء، ويسمى بخوارزم حق القرار ؛ لأنه نقلي كالأراضي السلطانية التي حازها السلطان لبيت المال ويدفعها مزارعة إلى الناس بالنصف فصار لهم فيها كردار كالبناء والأشجار والكيس بالتراب فبيعها باطل، وبيع الكردار إذا كان معلوما يجوز لكن لا شفعة فيه انتهى، ونحوه في "النهاية" و"الذخيرة" وفي "التاتارخانية" عن "السراجية : رجل له دار في أرض الوقف فلا شفعة له ولو باع هو داره فلا شفعة لجاره، انتهى، ومما يدل عليه قطعا ما في "الجامع الصغير" أن بيع أرض مكة لا يجوز وإنما يجوز بيع البناء فلا تجب الشفعة وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنها تجب، وهو قولهما وعليه الفتوى؛ لأنه باع المملوك، انتهى. قال في "شرح الوهبانية: ولا يخفى أن مفاد هذا الكلام أن الشفعة فيها إنما تثبت بناء على القول بأن أرضها مملوكة لا أن مجرد البناء فيها يوجب الشفعة فيكون حكمه مخالفا لغيره من الأبنية كما توهمه عبارة ابن وهبان، فإن عبارة ابن وهبان توهم أن ثبوت الشفعة فيها بمجرد البناء وليس كذلك بل ثبوتها خاص بالقول بملكية أرضها ليكون البناء تابعا للأرض فلا يكون من بيع المنقول، والعجب من أبي السعود حيث استدل بهذا الكلام وجعله صريحا فيما ادعاه مع أنه صريح بخلافه، وما في شرح المجمع فلا دليل فيه أيضا؛ لأن التعليل المذكور لبيان الفرق بين بيع البناء أو النخل And know that the building which has the right of permanence, like the building in waqf land that is leased and sultani land – does pre-emption become obligatory in it or not? There is lengthy discussion mentioned in al-Shami and Fath al-Mu'in. Its summary: Ibn al-Kamal asserted that when a building is sold with the right of permanence, like the building in sultani land and waqf, it joins real estate just like the upper floor joined. Abu al-Su'ud supported him in his commentary on al-Kanz and asserted the error of whoever gave the fatwa that there is no pre-emption in the building on leased land like al-Turi; for he has no basis in his fatwa. He argued with what is in Sharh al-Majma': if date palms alone or the building alone are sold, there is no pre-emption; because they are a reaffirmation of them without the ground. He said: his explanation is almost explicit in establishing pre-emption in the building on leased land because it has the right of permanence. He also argued with what was narrated by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from the Imam concerning the sale of a building in Makkah, as will be explained later. And he refuted Ibn al-Kamal, first by stating the difference between the mentioned building and the upper floor – that the building mentioned does not have the right to remain permanently, but is on the verge of vanishing, because they said: if the lessee of leased land refuses to pay the equivalent rent, he is ordered to remove his building and it is rented to someone else. Similarly for sultani lands if he refuses to pay what the sultan determined, unlike the right of the upper floor which remains permanently. And secondly by what is in al-Bazzaziyyah and others that there is no pre-emption in it. He said in al-Bazzaziyyah: there is no pre-emption in the kardar, i.e., the building, called in Khwarazm 'right of permanence', because it is movable like sultani lands which the sultan seized for the treasury and gives as sharecropping to people for half, so they have in it kardar like buildings and trees and the bag of soil; their sale is void, and the sale of kardar if known is permissible, but there is no pre-emption in it. End quote. Similar is in al-Nihayah and al-Dhakhirah, and in al-Tatarkhaniyyah from al-Sirajiyyah: a man has a house on waqf land – there is no pre-emption for him, and if he sells his house, there is no pre-emption for his neighbor. End quote. And what clearly indicates it is what is in al-Jami' al-Saghir: the sale of the land of Makkah is not permissible, and only the sale of the building is permissible, so pre-emption is not obligatory. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifah that it is obligatory, and that is their view, and the fatwa is upon it because he sold what is owned. End quote. He said in Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah: It is not hidden that the purport of this statement is that pre-emption in it is established only on the basis of the view that its land is owned, not that mere building in it entails pre-emption, so its ruling is contrary to other buildings, as is imagined from the wording of Ibn Wahban. For the wording of Ibn Wahban suggests that pre-emption in it is due to mere building, but it is not so; rather its establishment is specific to the view of ownership of its land so that the building follows the land and thus is not from the sale of movables. And it is strange from Abu al-Su'ud that he argued with this statement and made it explicit in his claim while it is explicit against it. And what is in Sharh al-Majma' is no evidence either, because the mentioned explanation is to clarify the difference between selling the building or date palms alone and selling them with their fixed location in which there is right of permanence permanently; then pre-emption is established due to the existence of permanent right of permanence, unlike the building or date palms alone, even on leased land as you have learned from what we have established. And it is possible that Ibn al-Kamal meant by 'right of permanence' the fixed location itself, so there is no conflict with others.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pre-emption in buildings erected on waqf land (leased) or sultani land (state land) where the builder has a right of permanence (haqq al-qarar). Ibn al-Kamal argued that such buildings should be treated like real estate (as the upper floor is) and thus pre-emption applies when sold with that right. Abu al-Su'ud supported this, criticizing the fatwa of al-Turi who said no pre-emption. However, the commentator refutes this by distinguishing: the right of permanence in these buildings is not permanent—if the lessee fails to pay rent, the building can be removed. The upper floor's right is permanent. Furthermore, al-Bazzaziyyah and other authoritative texts explicitly state no pre-emption in such cases (termed 'kardar' in Khwarazm). The text from al-Jami' al-Saghir about Makkah is also cited: the land is not owned, so only the building is sold, and no pre-emption applies. The narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad that Abu Hanifah said pre-emption is obligatory is interpreted as being based on the view that the land of Makkah is owned, not merely because of the building. Thus the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that there is no pre-emption in these buildings. This reflects the Hanafi principle that pre-emption is for immovable property, and buildings on non-owned land remain movable in legal consideration.

Key Terms
حق القرار (haqq al-qarar): Right of permanence: the right to keep a building on land owned by another (like waqf or state land) as long as rent is paid.
الكردار (al-kardar): A term used in Khwarazm for the right of permanence; essentially the building and its attached rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is pre-emption established in a building sold on waqf land with a right of permanence?
A) Yes, because the building is immovable
B) No, because the building is considered movable
C) Only if the buyer is the neighbor
D) Only if the land is owned

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that pre-emption does not apply to buildings on waqf or sultani land with right of permanence, as such buildings are considered movable property.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Pre-emption and Non-Sale Transactions
List of transactions where pre-emption does not apply, with reasoning and disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which transactions do not give rise to pre-emption in the Hanafi school and explain the rationale that pre-emption is limited to property-for-property exchanges.

Core Idea
Pre-emption only applies in pure property exchanges, not in compensations for marriage, hire, khul', blood money, manumission, gifts, or incomplete sales.

This classical Hanafi passage lists several transactions where pre-emption (shuf'ah) does not apply, despite the property being transferred. The common thread is that pre-emption is a right attached exclusively to the exchange of property for property (mu'awadat mal bi mal). The passage covers dower, rent, khul' compensation, blood money, manumission price, gifts without compensation, sales with seller's option, and invalid sales. It also discusses the intra-Hanafi difference regarding accidental blood money and the Shafi'i opposing view. By studying these rulings, you will understand how the Hanafi school restricts pre-emption to pure property sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Common Principle
Recognize that pre-emption is only established in a transaction that is a property-for-property exchange (mu'awadat mal bi mal). The Hanafi school holds that any transfer where the consideration is not property (or not purely property) does not trigger pre-emption.
This principle is derived from the wording: 'because pre-emption is legislated only for exchange of property for property'.
2
Categorize Each Listed Transaction
For each transaction in the passage (dower, rent, khul', blood money, manumission, gift without compensation, seller's option sale, invalid sale), determine whether it is a property-for-property exchange. Most are not; they are compensations for rights (marriage, hire, divorce, crime, freedom) or incomplete transfers.
Note the exceptions: gift with stipulated return is treated as a sale; buyer's option sale triggers pre-emption immediately; accidental blood money is treated as property exchange.
3
State the Ruling for Each Case
For each non-sale transaction, state that pre-emption is not established, and provide the reason: the consideration is not property in the context of pre-emption.
The reason is often that the Lawgiver made these compensations guaranteed by property due to necessity/gravity, but this does not extend to pre-emption.
4
Note Disputes and Exceptions
Account for the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on dual blood money (intentional + accidental): Abu Hanifah denies pre-emption entirely; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad grant it for the accidental portion. Also note the Shafi'i position that all these compensations are valuable, hence pre-emption applies in their school.
For seller's option, pre-emption becomes obligatory only after the option drops; for buyer's option, it is immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودار جعلت مهرا أو أجرة أو بدل خلع أو بدل صلح عن دم أو عوض عتق، أو وهبت بلا عوض مشروط، أو بيعت بخيار البائع، أو بيعت فاسدا، جعلت مهرا إلخ: يعني لا تحب أيضا في دار جعلت مهرا بأن تزوج عليها امرأة أو جعلت أجرة بأن استأجر حماما بدار، فدفعها إليه عوض الأجرة، أو جعلت بدل خلع بأن خالعها على دار دفعتها إليه، أو جعلت بدل صلح عن دم العمد، أو جعلت عوض عتق بأن أعتق عبده على دار وعند الشافعي: تجب فيه الشفعة؛ لأن هذه الأعواض متقومة عنده، وعندنا: لا؛ لأن المستحق بهذه العقود ليست بمال غير أن الشارع جعلها مضمونة بالمال، إما لخطرها أو للضرورة، فلا يتعدى موضعها، ولا ضرورة في حق ثبوت الشفعة، فلا تكون متقومة في حقه. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٢/٢] ولأن الشفيع يتملك بما يملك به المشتري من السبب لا بسبب آخر، وههنا لو أخذه كان يأخذه بسبب آخر. لو تزوجها بغير مهر ثم فرض لها عقارا مهرا لم يكن فيها الشفعة؛ لأنه تعين بمهر المثل، وهو مقابل بالبضع، بخلاف ما لو باعها العقار بمهر المثل أو بالمسمى عند العقد أو بعده حيث تثبت الشفعة؛ لأنه مبادلة مال بمال؛ لأن ما أعطاها من من المهر. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٧٣/٨] (الفتح) أو بدل صلح عن دم: أي عن دم" عمد ؛ لأنه لو كان عن دم خطأ تجب الشفعة، ولو صالح بها عن جنايتين: إحداهما عمد، والأخرى خطأ، فلا شفعة فيها على قول الإمام وعندهما تجب فيها الشفعة فيما يخص جناية الخطأ. [ تكملة البحر الرائق ۲۷۳/۸] أو عوض عتق لأن الشفعة إنما شرعت في معاوضة المال بالمال، وهذه العقود ليست كذلك، وصورته أن يقول لعبده : أعتقتك بدار فلان فوهب فلان الدار للعبد، فدفعها إلى المولى كذا في "الحموي".(فتح) أو وهبت إلخ: أي ولا تجب الشفعة في دار وهبت بلا عوض مشروط في العقد؛ لأنها ليست بمعاوضة مال بمال، وإنما قيد بقوله: "بلا عوض مشروط"؛ لأنه لو شرط العوض في العقد تجب الشفعة؛ لأنها بيع انتهاء لابد فيه من القبض، فإنه إذا وهب دارا لرجل على أن يهبه الآخر ألف درهم، فلا شفعة للشفيع ما لم يتقابضا وبعد التقابض تحب الشفعة بمثل عوض إن كان مثليا، وإلا فبقيمته إن كان قيميا ولو وهب له عقارا من غير عوض مشروط في العقد، ثم عوضه عن الدار دارا، فلا شفعة في الهبة، ولا في العوض؛ لأن كل واحد منهما هبة مطلقة. (فتح، تكملة) أو بيعت إلخ أي ولا تجب الشفعة في دار بيعت بخيار البائع؛ لأن خيار البائع يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملكه، فإذا أسقط الخيار أو سقط، الشفعة؛ لأن شرط وجوبه الخروج عن ملكه يمنع وجوب لزوال المانع عن خروج ملك البائع، ويشترط الطلب عند سقوط الخيار في الصحيح؛ لأن البيع يصير سببا عنده، وقيل: عند البيع وصحح أيضا، ولو كان الخيار للمشتري تجب الشفعة في الحال؛ لأن المبيع خرج عن ملك البائع وبقاء الخيار لا يمنع الشفعة.
And a dwelling made as dower, or hire, or compensation for khul', or compensation for blood money, or price of manumission, or gifted without a stipulated compensation, or sold with the option of the seller, or sold invalidly. 'Made as dower etc.': It means pre-emption is also not established in a dwelling made as dower, such as marrying a woman and giving it as dower; or made as hire, such as renting a bathhouse with a house, and he paid it to him as the hire price; or made as compensation for khul', such as he divorced her in exchange for a house she paid him; or made as compensation for blood money for intentional killing; or made as price of manumission, such as he freed his slave in exchange for a house. According to al-Shafi'i, pre-emption is obligatory because these compensations are valuable in his view. According to us, no, because what is due by these contracts is not property, but the Lawgiver made it guaranteed by property, either due to its gravity or necessity, so it does not extend beyond its place, and there is no necessity for establishing pre-emption, so they are not valuable for this purpose. And because the pre-emptor owns by the same cause as the buyer, not by another cause; and here if he took it, he would be taking it by another cause. If he married her without dower and then specified a property as dower, there is no pre-emption in it because it is determined by the dower of the like, and it is in exchange for the private part. Unlike if he sold her the property for the dower of the like or for the stipulated amount at the time of the contract or after, where pre-emption is established because it is exchange of property for property. 'Or compensation for blood money': i.e., for intentional blood money, because if it were for accidental blood money, pre-emption is obligatory. If he settled with it for two offenses, one intentional and the other accidental, then there is no pre-emption in it according to Imam Abu Hanifah, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), pre-emption is established in it for the portion of the accidental offense. 'Or price of manumission': because pre-emption is legislated only for exchange of property for property, and these contracts are not such. Its form: he says to his slave 'I free you in exchange for the house of so-and-so', so so-and-so gives the house to the slave, and he pays it to the master. 'Or gifted etc.': i.e., pre-emption is not established in a house gifted without a stipulated compensation in the contract, because it is not an exchange of property for property. He restricted it by saying 'without stipulated compensation' because if compensation is stipulated in the contract, pre-emption is obligatory because it is a sale. But delivery is necessary: if he gave a house to a man on condition that the other give him a thousand dirhams, there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor until they have taken possession; after taking possession, pre-emption is obligatory with the like of the compensation if it is quantifiable, otherwise with its value if it is unquantifiable. If he gifted him a property without stipulated compensation in the contract, and then compensated him for the house with a house, there is no pre-emption in the gift nor in the compensation, because each is an absolute gift. 'Or sold etc.': i.e., pre-emption is not established in a house sold with the option of the seller, because the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership. So when the option is dropped or expires, pre-emption becomes obligatory because the condition for its obligation is the removal from the seller's ownership, and the obstacle has been removed. And the demand is required at the time of the expiry of the option in the correct view, because the sale becomes a cause at that time. It was also said at the time of the sale, and this view was also deemed correct. And if the option were for the buyer, pre-emption is obligatory immediately because the sold item has left the seller's ownership, and the remaining option does not prevent pre-emption.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage lists several transactions where pre-emption (shuf'ah) does not apply, despite the conventional wisdom that pre-emption is a right in property sale. The common thread is that pre-emption is only established in a pure exchange of property for property (mu'awadat mal bi mal). The cases are: (1) a house given as dower (mahr) in marriage – this is not a sale but a nuptial gift; (2) as rent (ujrah) – the house is given for the right to use a bathhouse, not for property; (3) as compensation for khul' (badal khul') – the house is given for the wife's release from marriage; (4) as blood money (diyah) for intentional killing – the house compensates for a crime, not for property; (5) as price of manumission ('iwad 'itq) – the house is for freeing a slave; (6) a gift without stipulated return – it's a donation, not a sale; (7) a sale with seller's option (khiyar al-ba'i') – because ownership does not fully transfer until option is settled; (8) an invalid sale (mabi' fasid) – such a sale does not transfer ownership and thus no pre-emption arises. The passage also provides distinctions: for accidental homicide, pre-emption may apply because the compensation is treated as property. For a dual offense (intentional and accidental), Abu Hanifah says no pre-emption at all, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say pre-emption applies for the accidental portion. For the gift with stipulated return, it is effectively a sale, so pre-emption applies after possession. For seller's option, pre-emption only after the option drops; for buyer's option, pre-emption applies immediately because the property has already left the seller's ownership. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi principle that pre-emption is a right attached to property exchanges and not to other forms of transfers.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a partner or neighbor to purchase a property sold to a third party.
المهر (al-mahr): Dower: property given by husband to wife in marriage.
بدل الصلح عن دم العمد (badal al-sulh 'an dam al-'amd): Compensation for intentional homicide: settlement payment for murder.
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce by mutual consent where the wife pays compensation to the husband.
بدل الصلح عن دم الخطأ (badal al-sulh 'an dam al-khata): Compensation for accidental homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is pre-emption established in a house given as dower in marriage?
A) Yes, because it is a valuable property
B) No, because it is not an exchange of property for property
C) Only if the dower is more than the market value
D) Only if the marriage is later annulled

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption in the Hanafi school is restricted to pure property-for-property exchanges; it does not arise from compensations like dower, rent, khul', blood money, or manumission prices.

6 min
No Pre-emption for Property Given as Dower, Rent, etc.
Inter-madhhab dispute on shuf'ah for non-sale compensations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school denies shuf'ah for property given as dower or rent, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is not established for compensations that are not property — the Lawgiver's guarantee is context-specific.

This lesson addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). While both schools agree on the general concept of shuf'ah, they differ on whether it applies when a property is given as dower, rent, or other non-sale compensations. The Hanafi position, based on the nature of these compensations, denies shuf'ah, whereas the Shafi'i school treats them as valuable property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the ruling that shuf'ah is not established for property given as mahr, ujrah, khul' compensation, blood money, or manumission consideration. List each case.
The passage enumerates five cases.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
State that Imam al-Shafi'i holds shuf'ah is established because these compensations are valuable property (muttaqqawim).
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof 1
Understand the reasoning: these compensations are not property in themselves; the Lawgiver only made them guaranteed by property for specific reasons (seriousness or necessity), and this guarantee does not extend to pre-emption.
The key term is 'لا يتعدى موضعها' (it does not extend beyond its place).
4
Analyse the Hanafi proof 2
The shafi' (pre-emptor) must take ownership by the same cause as the buyer; here the buyer acquired through a non-sale cause (marriage, khul', etc.), so the shafi' cannot claim by a different cause.
This is a second independent proof.
5
Apply to an additional scenario
Consider the case where a man marries without dower and later assigns a property as dower: no shuf'ah, because it is determined as mahr al-mithl (proper dower). Contrast with selling the property for the dower, where shuf'ah applies.
This illustrates the principle that only a sale (exchange of property) triggers shuf'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودار جعلت مهرا أو أجرة أو بدل خلع أو بدل صلح عن دم أو عوض عتق، أي: لا تجب الشفعة في دار جعلت مهرا بأن تزوج عليها امرأة، أو جعلت أجرة بأن استأجر حماما بدار فدفعها إليه عوض الأجرة، أو جعلت بدل خلع بأن خالعها على دار دفعتها إليه، أو جعلت بدل صلح عن دم العمد، أو جعلت عوض عتق بأن أعتق عبده على دار. وعند الشافعي: تجب فيه الشفعة؛ لأن هذه الأعواض متقومة عنده، وعندنا: لا؛ لأن المستحق بهذه العقود ليست بمال غير أن الشارع جعلها مضمونة بالمال، إما لخطرها أو للضرورة، فلا يتعدى موضعها، ولا ضرورة في حق ثبوت الشفعة، فلا تكون متقومة في حقه. ولأن الشفيع يتملك بما يملك به المشتري من السبب لا بسبب آخر، وههنا لو أخذه كان يأخذه بسبب آخر. لو تزوجها بغير مهر ثم فرض لها عقارا مهرا لم يكن فيها الشفعة؛ لأنه تعين بمهر المثل، وهو مقابل بالبضع، بخلاف ما لو باعها العقار بمهر المثل أو بالمسمى عند العقد أو بعده حيث تثبت الشفعة؛ لأنه مبادلة مال بمال.
And a house made as dower, or rent, or compensation for khul', or compensation for blood money (of intentional killing), or consideration for manumission: shuf'ah is not established. That is, shuf'ah is not established in a house made as dower by marrying a woman upon it, or made as rent by renting a bathhouse for a house and paying it as rent, or made as compensation for khul' by entering into khul' upon a house and paying it to her, or made as compensation for blood money of intentional killing, or made as consideration for manumission by freeing his slave for a house. According to al-Shafi'i: shuf'ah is established because these compensations are valuable in his view. According to us: no, because what is owed by these contracts is not property; however, the Lawgiver made them guaranteed by property, either due to their seriousness or necessity, so it does not extend beyond its place, and there is no necessity for establishing shuf'ah, so they are not valuable in his right. And because the shafi' takes ownership by the same cause by which the buyer takes ownership, not by another cause; here if he takes it, he would take it by another cause. If he married her without a dower then prescribed a real estate as dower, there is no shuf'ah in it because it is determined as the proper dower, and it is in exchange for the vulva, unlike if he sold her the real estate for the proper dower or for the stipulated dower at the contract or after it, where shuf'ah is established because it is an exchange of property for property.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding shuf'ah (pre-emption) when property is given as dower, rent, khul' compensation, blood money compensation, or manumission consideration. The Hanafi position is that shuf'ah is not established in such cases because these compensations are not considered property (mal) in themselves; rather, the Lawgiver made them guaranteed by property for specific reasons (seriousness or necessity), but this guarantee does not extend to granting pre-emption rights. The Shafi'i school holds that such compensations are valuable property, hence pre-emption applies. The Hanafi reasoning is further supported by the principle that the shafi' takes ownership by the same cause as the buyer; here, the buyer acquired the property through a non-proprietary cause (e.g., marriage, khul'), so the shafi' cannot claim through a different cause. An additional scenario clarifies: if a man marries without specifying a dower and later assigns a property as dower, no shuf'ah arises because it becomes determined as the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) which is truly in exchange for the right to marital relations. But if he sells her the property for the stipulated dower, pre-emption applies as it is an exchange of property.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbour to purchase a property that has been sold to an outsider, by paying its price.
المهر (al-mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife in marriage; it is a type of compensation that is not considered property for pre-emption purposes.
العوض (al-'iwad): Consideration or compensation; in this context, something given in exchange that may or may not be property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is shuf'ah established for a house given as dower (mahr)?
A) Yes, because mahr is valuable property.
B) No, because mahr is not considered property for pre-emption.
C) It depends on whether the dower is specified at the time of contract.
D) Only if the house is given after the marriage contract.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, shuf'ah does not arise from compensations that are not genuine sales of property, such as dower or rent, because the cause of acquisition differs.

Shuf'ah and the Gift without Stipulated Compensation
Ruling on shuf'ah for a gift without stipulated compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'ah for a gift given without stipulated compensation and distinguish when a gift becomes a sale for pre-emption purposes.

Core Idea
A gift without stipulated compensation does not trigger shuf'ah; a gift with stipulated compensation is a sale.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on pre-emption (shuf'ah) in relation to gifts. While shuf'ah only applies to sales, a gift that includes a condition of compensation is legally treated as a sale. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a pre-emption right arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states: no shuf'ah for a house gifted without compensation. This is the baseline ruling.
The gift is not a sale, so no pre-emption.
2
Understand the restriction
The phrase 'without stipulated compensation' is key. If compensation is stipulated in the contract, the transaction is a sale, and shuf'ah applies.
The condition transforms the gift into a sale.
3
Note the possession condition
Even when compensation is stipulated, shuf'ah only arises after both parties take possession (qabd). Until then, no pre-emption.
Possession is required for the right to vest.
4
Treat the compensation type
If the compensation is mithli (fungible), shuf'ah is based on an equivalent; if qimi (non-fungible), on its value.
This affects the quantum of shuf'ah.
5
Separate unconditional gifts
If a gift is unconditional and later the recipient gives a separate property as compensation, neither transfer gives shuf'ah, as each is an absolute gift.
The compensation is a separate gift, not part of the original contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو وهبت إلخ: أي ولا تجب الشفعة في دار وهبت بلا عوض مشروط في العقد؛ لأنها ليست بمعاوضة مال بمال، وإنما قيد بقوله: 'بلا عوض مشروط'؛ لأنه لو شرط العوض في العقد تجب الشفعة؛ لأنها بيع، انتهاء لابد فيه من القبض، فإنه إذا وهب دارا لرجل على أن يهبه الآخر ألف درهم، فلا شفعة للشفيع ما لم يتقابضا، وبعد التقابض تجب الشفعة بمثل عوض إن كان مثليا، وإلا فبقيمته إن كان قيميا. ولو وهب له عقارا من غير عوض مشروط في العقد، ثم عوضه عن الدار دارا، فلا شفعة في الهبة، ولا في العوض؛ لأن كل واحد منهما هبة مطلقة.
Or was gifted without stipulated compensation: that is, shuf'ah is not established in a house gifted without a compensation stipulated in the contract, because it is not an exchange of property for property. He restricted with 'without stipulated compensation' because if compensation is stipulated in the contract, shuf'ah is established, because it is a sale, and ultimately delivery (qabd) is necessary. So if one gives a house to someone on condition that the other gives him a thousand dirhams, there is no shuf'ah for the shafi' until they take possession, and after taking possession, shuf'ah is established for an equivalent compensation if it is mithli, otherwise for its value if it is qimi. And if one gives real estate to him without a stipulated compensation in the contract, then compensates him for the house with a house, there is no shuf'ah in the gift nor in the compensation, because each one is an absolute gift.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a ruling: shuf'ah is not established for a house given as an unconditional gift, because it is not an exchange of property. The restriction 'without stipulated compensation' is crucial: if compensation is stipulated (even if called a gift), the transaction is effectively a sale and shuf'ah applies once both parties take possession. The compensation may be mithli (fungible) or qimi (non-fungible), affecting the value basis. If the gift is unconditional but later the recipient compensates the donor with another property, that compensation is itself an absolute gift, so no shuf'ah arises from either transfer.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation.
العوض المشروط (al-'iwad al-mashrut): Compensation stipulated in the contract; if present, the contract is treated as a sale for pre-emption purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person gives a house to another on condition that the recipient gives him a car, is shuf'ah established?
A) No, because it is a gift.
B) Yes, because the condition makes it a sale.
C) Only after delivery of the car.
D) No, because the car is not equivalent property.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah does not arise from a gift without compensation; only from sales or gift-contracts with stipulated compensation after possession.

No Pre-emption for Sale with Seller's Option
Ruling on shuf'ah timing with seller's option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for shuf'ah in a sale with seller's option and distinguish it from a sale with buyer's option.

Core Idea
Pre-emption (shuf'ah) hinges on ownership leaving the seller; a seller's option delays it, while a buyer's option does not.

This passage addresses a key nuance in pre-emption (shuf'ah) law: what happens when a property is sold with an option held by the seller? The Hanafi ruling is clear – shuf'ah is not established immediately because ownership does not fully transfer until the option lapses. We will dissect the reasoning and contrast it with the buyer's option scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between khiyar al-ba'i' (seller's option) and khiyar al-mushtari (buyer's option). Note the different rulings.
Shuf'ah is a right of the neighbor/partner to purchase the property.
2
Understand the underlying condition
Shuf'ah becomes obligatory (wajib) only when the sold property fully leaves the seller's ownership. This is the key principle.
Ownership transfer is central.
3
Apply to seller's option
The seller's option prevents the property from leaving his ownership. Therefore, shuf'ah is not wajib during the option period.
When the option is dropped or lapses, ownership leaves and shuf'ah becomes wajib.
4
Note the dispute on timing of demand
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the soundest view requires the pre-emptor to demand after the option lapses; another view allows demand at the time of sale.
The relied-upon view is the former.
5
Contrast with buyer's option
When the buyer has the option, ownership immediately leaves the seller (though the buyer can rescind). Hence shuf'ah is wajib at once.
The buyer's option does not affect the seller's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بيعت إلخ أي ولا تجب الشفعة في دار بيعت بخيار البائع؛ لأن خيار البائع يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملكه، فإذا أسقط الخيار أو سقط، وجبت الشفعة؛ لأن شرط وجوبها الخروج عن ملكه يمنع وجوبها لزوال المانع عند خروج ملك البائع، ويشترط الطلب عند سقوط الخيار في الصحيح؛ لأن البيع يصير سببا عنده، وقيل: عند البيع وصحح أيضا. ولو كان الخيار للمشتري تجب الشفعة في الحال؛ لأن المبيع خرج عن ملك البائع.
Or was sold with seller's option: that is, shuf'ah is not established in a house sold with an option for the seller, because the seller's option prevents the sold property from leaving his ownership. So when he drops the option or it lapses, shuf'ah is established, because the condition for its obligation is the departure from his ownership; the presence of the option prevents obligation due to the removal of the impediment when the seller's ownership leaves. It is required to make the demand (talab) at the time the option is dropped in the correct view, because the sale becomes a cause at that time. Some said at the time of the sale, and that is also corrected. And if the option is for the buyer, shuf'ah is established immediately because the sold property has left the seller's ownership.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes between seller's option (khiyar al-ba'i') and buyer's option. When the seller has the option to rescind, ownership does not fully leave him, so pre-emption is not established immediately. Once the option is dropped or lapses, ownership leaves, and shuf'ah becomes obligatory. The demand for pre-emption must be made after the option lapses in the soundest view. If the buyer has the option, ownership passes immediately from the seller (though the buyer can rescind), so pre-emption takes effect at once.

Key Terms
خيار البائع (khiyar al-ba'i'): The seller's option to rescind the sale within a stipulated period.
خيار المشتري (khiyar al-mushtari): The buyer's option to rescind the sale; ownership transfers immediately from the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale with seller's option, when does pre-emption become obligatory?
A) Immediately at the time of sale.
B) When the seller drops the option or it lapses.
C) Only after delivery of the property.
D) It never becomes obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption (shuf'ah) requires the property to leave the seller's ownership; a seller's option delays that, so shuf'ah becomes obligatory only when the option lapses.
